 MSc Thesis 
 
 
Computer modelling of mutations in 
neurodegenerative diseases 
 
30 ECTS by Kenny Emil Aunstrup, s122969 
Supervision 
* 
Professor Kasper Planeta Kepp 
Department of DTU chemistry 
Technical University of Denmark 
* 
March 2020 
 
 
==PAGE:2==
 
2 
 
 
Abstract 
Neurodegenerative disorders (NDDs) affects many people worldwide and are associated with loss of 
neuronal populations. No disease mechanisms are available for these diseases. In this project, a hypothesis 
for a universal disease mechanism are investigated. The hypothesis states that loss of stability of NDD-
associated proteins contribute to disease. This is investigated through the study of 5 proteins, PS1, 
Superoxide dismutase, Parkin, Prion Protein, and Tau, and their associated familiar disease forms, 
Alzheimer’s disease, Amyotrophic lateral sclerosis, Parkinson’s disease, Creutzfeldt Jakob Disease and 
Frontotemporal dementia respectively. By using the computational ΔΔG estimating methods, FoldX, 
Popmusic and I-Mutant3.0, to study ΔΔG upon point mutations for clinical mutations, it is found that 
decreased stability of PS-1 and superoxide dismutase are causing the associated NDDs, with significant p-
values, compared to mutations in non-conserved residues of the proteins. The results are reliable because 
the ΔΔG estimating methods are very different in the way they are constructed, but still show the same 
result on different pdb-structures and on control sets with a different degree of conserveness. 
Furthermore, the many mutations are spread out across the hole proteins, i.e. the mutations do not only lie 
in certain functional sites.  Parkin do also seem to underlie the hypothesis, although the correlation is not 
as strong as for PS-1 and SOD1. Decreased stability upon mutations for the Intrinsically disordered proteins 
(IDP) Prion and Tau are not correlated with disease, but the results might suffer from the lack of well-
defined pdb-structures. Overall, loss of stability of globular and membrane proteins seems to be causing 
NDD, but for IDP’s this is not the case. If loss of stability is causing NDD for all NDD-associated globular and 
membrane proteins, oxidative stress could contribute to loss of protein stability in sporadic forms of the 
diseases and be the disease mechanism for sporadic forms of NDDs.

3 
 
Preface 
The report is the result of a 30 ECTS master thesis project offered by the Department of Chemistry at the 
Technical University of Denmark (DTU). The project has spanned over a period of 6 months  August 29. 
2019 to March 08, 2020. One extra month was provided by the Department of Chemistry due to a 
simultaneous course at DTU, and due to 3 weeks of disease. The master thesis is the final thesis in the MSc 
program Advanced and Applied Chemistry.  The research described in this project was supervised by 
Professor Kasper Planeta Keep from the Department of Chemistry at DTU.  
The report is based on a study of 5 proteins, associated with 5 neurodegenerative disorders. 
I would like to thank Kasper Planeta for his big help and support during the project. He has provided a huge 
amount of useful information and offered his help by email and personal discussions as much as I needed. 
He has provided a lot of useful inputs and suggestions to improvements and provided the main hypothesis 
of the project. 
 
 
==PAGE:4==
 
4 
 
 
Problem statement 
The pathogenesis of neurodegenerative diseases has not yet been discovered, despite intensive research. 
The main goal of this project is to test the hypothesis that the destabilization of proteins contributes to 
neurodegenerative diseases. This would provide a common disease mechanism for the disorders and explain 
why neurodegenerative diseases are associated with misfolded and aggregates proteins, because 
destabilization of proteins contributes to misfolding and protein aggregation.  
This project hypothesis will be studied through analyzing clinical point mutations associated with familial 
forms of the neurodegenerative diseases. This will be done for 5 proteins, in which certain mutations in them 
are seen in individuals with the particular disorder. These 5 proteins with the associated diseases are: 
Superoxide Dismutase[Cu-Zn] (Amyotrophic lateral sclerosis), Parkin protein (Parkinson disease), PS1 
(Alzheimer’s disease), Prion protein PrP (Creutzfeldt–Jakob disease) and Tau (Frontotemporal Dementia).  
A database of proteins with single amino acids mutations, found in individuals suffering from the 5 
neurodegenerative diseases is going to be created. For all these clinical protein mutants the change in protein 
stability ∆∆𝐺 caused by the point mutation, will be calculated using 3 webservers: I-mutant 3.0, PoPmusic 
and FoldX. These protein changes will be compared to different control sets including mutations in non-
conserved residues of the proteins. Only non-conserved residues are considered, because mutations in 
conserved residues are so destabilizing and pathogenic for the proteins that that they are sorted out through 
evolution.  A statistical t-test will be performed, in order to investigate whether the mean value of the clinical 
point mutations is statistically significantly larger than the mean value of the mutations in non-conserved 
residues. If it is true that disease associated point mutations are more destabilizing than other mutations,  
the hypothesis can be accepted. Otherwise, a rejection of the hypothesis, tells that another disease 
mechanism is present.  
The 3 web servers are very diverse in their way of  a calculation ΔΔG, so if a consensus is seen between the 
3 methods, the results are reliable. Furthermore, the analysis is conducted on 3 different pdb-structures for 
each protein, to test the structure dependency of the methods. I.e. a goal of the thesis is to find effective 
methods and control data sets. And to test the sensitivity of the results based on the ΔΔG computing 
methods, the control set and the protein structure. Furthermore, to test whether the hypothesis withstand 
for control sets of diverse conserves, the conserveness of the control sets are variated. To put it briefly, a 
goal is to find effective methods and control sets, and to test the sensitivity of results based on the ΔΔG 
methods, the control sets and the protein structure.  
It will also be investigated whether a combination of the 3 ΔΔG methods, yields better results. The idea is 
that the 3 methods are so different, so that a combination of them with a more complex model, might benefit 
from extracting the most describing parts from each model.  
The pathogenicity of the mutations associated with familiar forms of the disorders are a variating and 
sometimes uncertain concept. The pathogenicity of the point mutations is hard to justify, but several 
methods can be used, such as estimates from different pathogenic mutation predictor programs. These 
estimates are used to justify the pathogenicity of the clinical Parkin mutations.   
If it can be concluded that familial forms of the diseases are due to destabilization of proteins  from point 
mutations, this can be extracted to the more prevalent sporadic forms of the disorders. For these disorders, 
oxidative stress highly contributes to decreased protein stability of the proteins, and thereby the decreased 
==PAGE:5==
 
5 
 
stability due to oxidative stress could be the disease mechanism in the sporadic cases. Ultimately, this could 
help developing targets for new drug designs, both in the familial and sporadic forms of the diseases.

1 Introduction 
 
Neurodegenerative diseases are representing a big threat to human health and are very debilitating for the 
patient and relatives. World health organization (WHO) has predicted neurodegenerative diseases to affect 
more than 70 million patients in 2030 and more than 106 million in 20501. The increase in aging population 
in the world, are the major factor of the increasing number of diseases per year2.  They are characterized by 
progressive neuronal dysfunction and death of certain neuronal cell populations3 and share some common 
features such as the appearance late in life, aggregated protein deposits, metal ion disorder, oxidative stress4. 
Examples include, Alzheimer’s disease, Parkinson disease, Amyotrophic lateral sclerosis, Creutzfeldt Jacob’s 
disease and Frontotemporal Degeneration which are the topic in this project. Alzheimer’s disease is the most 
prevalent one of all neurodegenerative diseases followed by Parkinson disease.  
Most cases of the disorder are sporadic, i.e. having no family history. The amount of sporadic cases variates 
in different NDD, with up to 95% sporadic as seen in ALS5 and AD6. Very little is known about the pathogenesis 
of sporadic neurodegenerative diseases and they are hard to study. Familial neurodegenerative disease 
provides a way to study NDDs by the study of associated disease genes, and hopefully the knowledge of 
pathogenic mechanism in familial forms can provide knowledge to the sporadic cases as well. 
Most neurodegenerative diseases involve misfolding of proteins7. Misfolded proteins undetected by the cell’s 
quality control system, tends to form aggregates seen in the brains of the patients with the diseases7. Definite 
diagnosis of the NDDs includes identification of specific proteins in the protein aggregates, also in the 
sporadic case. These aggregates are rich in β-sheets with hydrogen and hydrophobic bonding. Misfolding of 
proteins are caused by many factors such as ageing, somatic or genic mutations, changes in intracellular 
environment such as temperature, pH, oxidative stress and presence of metal ions 8.  
Age is a major risk factor in the diseases, as the diseases typically strikes elder people in the sporadic cases, 
almost always with a late age of onset above 60 years9. Roughly 30% of people above 85 years have 
Alzheimer’s Disease, and more cases are seen with an increase in age9. However, familial neurodegenerative 
diseases are typically associated with an early age of onset before the age of 60. Other than the main risk 
factor age, a large amount of genetic-, lifestyle- and environmental related risk factors for neurodegenerative 
diseases exist. E.g. poor education, smoking, depression, vitamin deficiencies, high alcohol intake, low 
physical activity and chemical exposure 10, with age being the main risk actor 10.  
In the familial forms of the diseases, specific mutations in certain genes are so pathogenic to always generate 
the disease, and inheritance of these mutations causes the same disease in the offspring. Furthermore, the 
proteins subject to these pathogenic mutations, are often the proteins seen in the aggregates. Therefore, 
mutation genes and neurodegenerative disease are undoubtedly connected, and certain protein mutations 
are causal (pathogenic) for certain neurodegenerative diseases11.  
Neurodegenerative diseases are complex disorders involving a wide range of clinical, cellular and biochemical 
manifestations with an unexplained connection, despite intensive research 12. Both common and individual 
disease hypothesis for the disease mechanism has arisen. The common ones for all the neurodegenerative 
diseases are e.g. oxidative stress, mitochondrial dysfunction, toxicity of misfolded proteins, failure of 
maintaining protein homeostasis, neuroinflammation, axonal transport deficits, calcium deregulation and 
DNA damage13. Currently there exist no cure for any of the neurodegenerative diseases, although medicine 
to lower symptoms exist for some of them, often with major side effects 2. 
==PAGE:10==
 
10 
 
Different clinical symptoms and loss of different neuronal populations are seen in the different diseases 4. 
E.g. in Parkinson’s disease, loss of neurons in the motor systems responsible for movement results in 
symptoms such as shaking of the hands.  
This project aims to investigate a hypothesis for a common neurodegenerative disease mechanism: “That 
decreased protein stability contributes to neurodegenerative diseases”. This hypothesis is investigated 
through the study of familial forms of the diseases. A dataset of clinical point mutations seen in the familial 
forms of the disorders, are compared to a data set of all other mutations in non-conserved mutation sites, 
to study whether pathogenic mutations are more destabilizing than mutations in non-conserved sites, in the 
general case for 5 different neurodegenerative diseases. The change in stability upon point mutation, are 
estimated by the 3 webservers FoldX, Popmusic and I-Mutant 3.0.  
Protein structures are highly optimized through evolution, and therefore point mutations most often 
destabilize the proteins. Only non-conserved sites are targeted, because mutations in conserved sites most 
likely are so pathogenic that people are not going to become old enough to obtain the neurodegenerative 
disease anyways. I.e. mutations in conserved sites are sorted out through evolution. 
The sensitivity of the results is tested according to: 
i. 
Choice of stability prediction method  
ii. 
Choice of control set 
iii. 
 Choice of protein structure 
If it is true that destabilized proteins due to point mutations are the reason for familial forms of the diseases, 
then it is highly possible that destabilized proteins from e.g. aging and oxidative damage, is the disease 
mechanism in the sporadic forms of the diseases as well. I.e. a new area of therapeutic drugs aiming at 
stabilizing the proteins such that they don’t misfold, but can perform their normal function, could arise.  
 
2 Background 
 
2.1 The 5 chosen neurodegenerative diseases 
 
Both genetic risk factors and mutations in single genes can lead to the 5 neurodegenerative diseases. Generic 
risk factors are mutations in a gene or a certain allele of a gene, that increases the risk of developing the 
disease, but is not sufficiently pathogenic to be causal of the disease. An important genetic risk factor for 
Alzheimer’s disease is the E-4 allele of the APOE gene, which increases the risk of developing AD and 
decreases the age of onset14. The E-4 APOE allele constitutes 20% to 30% of the general population15. Genetic 
risk factors are out of scope of this project and will not be further addressed.  
Monogenic NNDs are caused by a mutation in a single gene. The following sections list tables of monogenic 
NDDs and some clinical facts about the 5 neurodegenerative diseases. As new pathogenic gene loci are found 
in novel research, these tables might not be complete and is constantly developing. AD means autosomal 
dominant, while AR means autosomal recessive.  
 
==PAGE:11==
 
11 
 
Alzheimer’s disease 
Alzheimer’s disease is the most common type of NDD, and accounts for 50% to 75% of all NDDs11. 
Approximately 30-35 million individuals have AD world-wide16. Alzheimer’s disease is seen as progressive 
memory loss and cognitive impairments 17. In Alzheimer’s disease the loss of neurons takes place primarily 
in the cerebral cortex and in the hippocampus, responsible for memory, language, consciousness and 
attention, thus leading to the loss of cognitive skills, identity, and activity seen in individuals with AD 17. 
Around 95% are sporadic and the rest are familial 11. Two type of aggregates are seen. Intracellular senile 
plaques composed of oxidized and metal-bound β-amyloid peptides. And neurofibrillary tangles mainly 
composed of hyperphosphylated tau 12. 
3 genes associated with familial Alzheimer’s disease has been found, and these are shown in Table 1

ALS 
Amyotrophic lateral sclerosis (ALS), also called motor neuron disease, is characterized by death of 
glutamatergic (using glutamate as neurotransmitter) and cholinergic (using acetylcholine as 
neurotransmitter) motor neurons, in the spinal cord and cortex of the brain 18. Motor neurons control muscle 
movement. Therefore, the clinical symptoms of ALS includes muscle weakness, loss of muscle mass and can 
eventually lead to inability of controlling muscles 19.   Above 160 different mutations in the SOD1 are reported 
to be associated with ALS, most of these are point mutations 20. This makes SOD1 the gene with most familial 
ALS associated mutations. Approximately 12% of individuals with FALS are associated with mutations in 
SOD1. A repeat in the C9ORF72 gene are the most common gene variant associated with FALS, found in about 
40% of FALS. Approximately 20 percent of the persons with ALS also develop FTD 21, in which the disease are 
called ALS-FTD. Approximately 95% of the total cases of ALS5 are sporadic, rest is familial.  
The protein aggregates, which is located in the cytoplasm of motor neurons and surrounding  
oligodendrocytes22, can consist of most of the proteins seen in Table 2 and more. An overview of the 
molecular components of the ALS aggregates in different FALS can be seen in 23. However, superoxide 
dismutase is only seen in FALS with SOD1 associated mutations, and most often not in sporadic ALS 23.  
Approximately 15 genes are associated with FALS, and some of them are shown in Table 2. More genes are 
found in reference 24.

2.1.1 CJD 
CJD is a are neurodegenerative disorder, with 1-2 million new cases seen worldwide 37. FCJD accounts for 10-
15% while sporadic account for 85-90%37.  
Only mutations in the PRNP gene coding for the Major Prion protein, are seen to be associated with FCJD. 
The prion gene are located at 20p13.  
CJD belongs to a family of diseases called prion diseases or transmissible spongiform encephalopathies, which 
can cause diseases both in human and animals 38.  Other prion diseases are e.g. Gerstmann-Sträussler-
Scheinker syndrome (GSS), Fatal insomnia (FI) and Kuru. Clinical symptoms of CJD are most often progressive 
dementia, and different other symptoms are seen in individuals, such as muscle difficulties and impaired 
vision. Prion diseases including CJD are the only disease capable of be transmitted to other people or animals 
37. CJD and the other prion diseases are autosomal inherited. 85-90% of all CJD’s cases are sporadic39.  
The protein aggregates in CJD consist of misfolded Prion protein. This misfolded Prion protein have been 
given the abbreviation PrPSc, i.e. the scrapie isoform of Prion. The correctly folded cellular form of Prion is 
referred to as PrPC.  PrPSc can cause PrPC to unfold and aggregate.  
 
2.1.2 PD 
Parkinson disease is the second most common neurodegenerative disorder after AD. More than 10 million 
people have PD worldwide 40.  85-90% cases are sporadic, rest is familial41. PD is seen as dysfunction and loss 
of dopamine producing neurons of the substantia nigra, leading to dopamine depletion. Clinical symptoms 
are seen at a loss of 50-60% of the dopaminergic neurons within the substantia nigra 42. Dopamine plays a 
role in reward and movement. The substantia nigra is located in the midbrain and also plays a role in reward 
and movement. 
Clinical symptoms of Parkinson include impaired movement, weakness of muscles, shaking and difficulty with 
walking and talking 17. The protein aggregates are called Lewy bodies, resides intracellular and consist mainly 
of the α-synuclein protein 43. In vitro models of PD have informed both apotosis and necrosis cell death of 
neurons42.  
Several gene loci has been associated with FPD and some of them are shown in Table 3.

Frontotemporal dementia (FTD) is the second most abundant type of dementia, after Alzheimer’s disease. 
50-70% of FTD are sporadic, rest is familial 60. Neuronal loss is seen in the frontal and anterior temporal cortex 
of the brain 61.  
Frontotemporal lobar degeneration (FTLD) is the general pathology of FTD, because neurons loss is seen in 
the frontal- and temporal lobe of the brain. FTLD can be subdivided into three different pathologies: FTLD-
tau, FTLD-TDP-43 and FTLD-FUS, dependent on the protein seen in the aggregates: Tau, TDP-43 (or ubiquitin) 
or FUS respectively. FTLD-Tau accounts for approximately 40% of all FTLD and have hyperphosphorylated tau 
inclusions in neurons and glia. Most clinical cases are however associated with mutations in genes causing 
FTLD-TDP43 with aggregates of ubiquitin and TDP-43 proteins.  However, tau is the protein with most 
confirmed pathogenic familial FTD mutations, and therefore tau and FTD-tau are the subject of this project.   
==PAGE:14==
 
14 
 
Familial FTLD-tau is most often caused by mutations in the MAPT gene for tau62. FTLD-tau includes several 
diseases, e.g. Pick’s disease (3R), Progressive supranuclear palsy, FTD with parkinsonism and corticobasal 
degeneration (4R), in which the main tau-isoform in the including varieties, i.e. 3R and 4R for three and four 
repeats of the microtubule binding domain respectively. These diseases also variates in the brain area 
affected and neuron type affected, and in their clinical feature 62. All FTLD-tau diseases caused by mutations 
in MAPT are included in this project. 
Furthermore, FTLD-tau is a subclass of tauopathies, characterized by neuronal and/or glial inclusion 
composed of tau 63. Alzheimer’s disease and down syndrome are other examples of tauopathies. 
Cell death may be caused by either apoptosis or necrosis 61. 
 
 
2.2 Central nervous system in relation to neurodegenerative disorders 
 
Neurodegenerative disorders are caused by gradual increasing dysfunction or death of certain neuronal 
populations of the brain 17. Neurons are cells of the central nervous system that transports signals to and 
from the entire body. All neurons consist of a cell body (soma), multiple dendrites and an axon. Many types 
of neurons exist. A simple neuron with the basic units, are shown in Figure 1.  
 
Figure 1 – A neuron with the most basic units. Picture modified from 64. The centrosome organelle organizes the microtubules 64. 
Microtubules is found in all axons, which is aligned along the axon and have a uniform polarity with plus ends 
towards the axon terminal. Microtubules are a main part of the cytoskeleton and provides structural stability 
to the axon 17. In the axon they are found in loose groupings along the axon where they vary in spacings.17 
Microtubules are long hollow polymers built from heterodimers of α- and β-tubulin, in an alternating manner 
as seen on Figure 2.  
==PAGE:15==
 
15 
 
 
 
Figure 2  - Left: A model of a microtubule. Picture from:  https://biologydictionary.net/microtubule/ .  
Right: Dimer of α- and β-tubulin from pdb: 1jff, created by Pymol. β-tubulin subunit in cyan. α-subunit colored by secondary 
structure in green, red and yellow. GDP and GTP shown in magenta. Magnesium ion in blue. Zinc ion in green. Taxol molecule in 
orange, to stabilize structure.  
The α- and β-tubulin dimer are shown to the right in Figure 2. α- and β-tubulin differ by a Asp254 and Lys254 
respectively 65. 
Microtubules also guide intracellular transport and cause changes in shape during synaptic functions66 , in 
which they bind to mitochondria and vesicles. Microtubules are also found in the dendrites.  
Microtubules are highly dynamic structures, that is they assemble (called growth or microtubule rescue) and 
dissemble (called shrinkage or microtubule catastrophe) all the time, by the addition and removal of the 
heterodimers. This microtubule dynamics behavior is assisted and regulated by many different molecules 
and PTM’s. One of these are MAPs (microtubule associated proteins), functioning as a regulator for the 
dynamic behavior of MT 67. The regulation the microtubules dynamics are important for the MT function in 
the cell 67. Tau and MAP2 are examples of MAPs. The α- and β-tubulin dimer can bind to GTP (Guanosine 
triphosphate) and GDP (Guanosine diphosphate). GTP promotes assembly of microtubules, while GDP 
promotes disassembly 68. GTP is always attached to the α-tubulin subunit, while GDP is attached to the β-
tubulin subunit  68. 
 
Cell deaths are at least performed by three mechanisms in neurodegenerative disorders. These are apoptosis, 
necrosis and autophagy.  
Apoptosis is initiated by the cell it-self. The cellular content is isolated by membranes and are consumed by 
macrophages, and the cellular ATP is maintained 69.  
Necrosis is a form of cell death in which the plasma membrane rapidly breaks down in response to certain 
signals, and then the cellular components are lost into the extraocular space and can cause inflammation. 
The cellular ATP is lost as well. Necrosis is usually caused by severe cellular stress, e.g. high levels of toxins 70. 
Autophagy is usually the mechanism by which organelles, protein aggregates and some proteins are 
degraded by the cells (see section later). But markers of autophagy has also been seen during cell death 70. 
 
 
==PAGE:16==
 
16 
 
 
2.3 Protein folding 
Proteins must be folded into specific 3D structures, to be functional. The primary structure of a protein 
determines its structure, and the structure determines its function71, at least for non-IDPs.  Some proteins 
can fold themselves, but others need help of molecular structures such as chaperones 72. It is important for 
proteins to fold correctly, to obtain their functionality, and to avoid toxic effect of misfolding. It is necessary 
to understand regular protein folding, in order to get deeper into protein misfolding.   
 
2.3.1 Regular protein folding 
The final tertiary structure of most proteins is usually the most thermodynamically stable structure under 
psychological conditions73. Evidence suggest that proteins fold in a trial and error process, 72 but a systematic 
trial and error search for the particular native structure will take a huge amount of time because there is an 
enormous amount of possible configurations an polypeptide can have during folding 74. 
Typically it takes few milliseconds of a small sized protein to fold 72, and thus it is hard to analyze by 
computers. A model of protein folding is the free energy landscape. It shows that many different trajectories 
are possible from the random coil, to the specific 3D structure, involving different possible intermediates. I.e. 
the stochastic trial and error folding process follows different possible trajectories, and thus are not 
systematic. Native-like interactions are more stable than non-native like, and therefore native-like 
interactions remain during the hole folding process while non-native interactions goes apart. The energy 
landscape is defined by the interactions between residues72, in which the free energy decreases with respect 
to the number of native interactions and the number of residue contacts, as seen in seen in Figure 3. The 
different folding trajectories and intermediates be determined experimentally, for example through NMR75 
A key step in the folding process is the formation of a “folding nucleus” (also called the transition state) which 
involves the interaction of a small number of residues. In the transition state, a couple of residues has made 
some key interactions, and the transition state has the same overall topology as the native fold, however 
with a high degree of disorder. When this nucleus is formed, the remainder of the structure rapid forms 
around this nucleus, because the native structure is more energetically favorable than the nucleus72. For 
smaller proteins, one transition state happens. For larger proteins more than one transition state can happen, 
although it is not clear whether some of the transitions state are traps that inhibit the folding process.  
Larger proteins fold in modules, i.e. different part of the protein can fold separately of the other parts and 
domains. 72 
 
==PAGE:17==
 
17 
 
 
Figure 3 – A schematic protein landscape for protein folding. Picture taken from 74 
The folding either takes place during DNA synthesis in which the some of the protein chain still is attached to 
the ribosome. In the cytoplasm, after release from the ribosome. Or in the mitochondria or ER 74. 
Many proteins have shown to refold to the native form from a denatured state, given that the protein is not 
too large72.  
 
2.3.2 Chaperones 
In order to increase the number of correctly folded proteins in the cell, the cell uses chaperones to assist in 
protein folding. I.e. because proteins or at least parts of the proteins are folded in the aqueous solvent, the 
chaperones help to avoid unfavorable interactions, that could lead to protein aggregations 76. Protein 
aggregation are further avoided by chaperones, because they block intermolecular interactions and stabilizes 
native intramolecular interactions. Chaperones can recognize misfolded proteins and refold them77. 
Different families of chaperones exist, each with different purposes. An important family of chaperones is 
the “heat shick proteins” (HSPs). Two examples are Hsp70 and Hsp60. HSP have increased expression at high 
temperatures78.   
 
2.3.3 Misfolding of proteins  
The difference in energy between the folded and unfolded state is quite small, because a significant loss of 
entropy happens when the protein folds. This also means that proteins are very fragile to changes in 
temperature, pH and chemicals, in which the weak interactions (hydrophobic, HB, ionic) in the proteins 
break, and the protein can unfold79. Therefore, proteins can unfold by many reasons, including: Dysfunction 
==PAGE:18==
 
18 
 
of chaperones. If the “folding nucleus” described earlier are not formed. Abnormal post-translational 
modifications, such as phosphorylation, glycosylation and metal-binding. Due to thermal, pH or oxidative 
damage. Due to mutations in proteins, both generic mutations or mutations from undetected errors made 
by the protein synthesis machinery 80,81. Most of these factors can change the free energy of the protein, and 
if the protein is destabilized, the protein might misfold.  
The loss of specific 3D structure upon misfolded, can lead to monomeric or oligomeric conformational 
ensembles, or larger proteins aggregates depending on the denaturing conditions 82. Misfolded proteins 
cause harm to the cell in different possible ways. These includes protein oligomerization and protein 
aggregates, which are thought to be toxic, non-detected dysfunctional proteins that performs incorrect 
functions and usage of energy to degrade misfolded proteins resulting in depletion of energy for other 
cellular processes. Failure of prober protein folding or failure to remain proper folded is associated with 
pathological conditions, such as late onset diabetes, cystic fibrosis and neurodegenerative disorders. Such 
disorders are also called conformational diseases or protein misfolding diseases, as they are characterized by 
misfolding, conformational changes and aggregation of a protein 83. In most of these disorders, proteins self-
assemble in an atypical way into large molecular aggregates, called amyloid fibrils.   
 
2.3.4 Protein aggregation 
When proteins misfold, they tend to aggregate together. When non IDP’s misfold, the hydrophobic core is 
subjected to the aqueous environment, which is very unfavorable, and therefore the proteins tend to lower 
their free surface energy by aggregating together forming hydrophobic intermolecular interactions 84. I.e. 
they polymerize into aggregates and organized fibrils. NMR has successively confirmed that aggregates are 
rich in beta-sheets 4. This aggregated sate, also called the amyloid state, is a generic state which in principle 
is accessible to all kind of proteins. More than 20 different proteins have been associated with amyloid fibrils 
and disease85.  
Both aggregates and the native state of the protein, are stable structures, as shown in Figure 4. The 
aggregates can either accumulate intra- or extracellularly, dependent on the protein and the 
neurodegenerative disease.  
 
==PAGE:19==
 
19 
 
Figure 4 – Free energy of different proteins states. Both aggregates and the native protein state are thermodynamically stable 
structures. Figure from 86 
When these aggregates are formed, they are favored due to the large activation energy that has to be 
overcome, when going from the aggregates towards the folded state. 
Protein aggregates are typically formed from partially folded conformations or protein oligomers. It has been 
suggested that the initial formation of protein oligomers, makes other proteins very favorable to interact 
with these and misfold. This model is called nucleation-dependent polymerization model of amyloid 
formation 87. When the protein concentration exceeds a critical concentration, oligomers are formed due to 
unfavorable interactions between monomers. These oligomers have an ordered nucleus, which catalyze the 
further growth of polymers.4 I.e. the initial formation of oligomers, acts as seeds to misfold more proteins 
and the equilibrium are switched towards the aggregated state 79. 
 
2.4 Protein degradation 
The amount of proteins in the cells are both determined by the rate of synthesis and the rate of degradation. 
Several types of proteins are highly degraded: 
Many proteins functioning as regulatory molecules, such as a lot of transcription factors, are often highly 
synthesized and degraded, as concentrations of these in the cell control the expression of genes.  
Furthermore, a lot of proteins, especially enzymes, are degraded in response to specific signals. Misfolded 
proteins are recognized and degraded. Lastly in cellular starving, proteins can be degraded to amino acids to 
provide cellular energy and amino acids for gluconeogenesis and new protein synthesis 88.  
Two major protein degradation pathways exist in eukaryotic cells: The ubiquitin proteasome pathway and 
the lysosomal proteolysis 89. 
 
2.4.1 The ubiquitin-proteasome pathway 
The ubiquitin-proteasome pathway uses the ubiquitin molecule to mark cytosolic and nuclear protein as 
target for rapid proteolysis (proteolysis is the degradation of proteins into peptide chains) 89.  
The family of enzymes called “ubiquitin ligase or E3” recognize different substrate proteins for degradation. 
Before the attachment of ubiquitin to the target protein molecule, ubiquitin needs to be activated. This is 
done by attachment of ubiquitin to the “ubiquitin-activating enzyme, E1”. After this activation, ubiquitin is 
transferred to a second enzyme called “ubiquitin-conjugating enzyme E2”. And finally, ubiquitin is then 
catalytically transferred to a lysine at the C-terminal of the target protein molecule by E3 88. This multistep 
pathway is shown in Figure 5. Often E3 binds both E2 and the substrate. 
Multiple ubiquitin molecule is added by this pathway. The polyubiquitinated protein are then recognized and 
moved to a multisubunit protease complex called 26S proteasome 88, for degradation. The peptides are then 
further degraded to amino acids by peptidases or used in antigen presentation 88. During the degradation, 
the ubiquitin molecules are released, and can be used again. ATP is required both in the attachment and 
degradation of the marked proteins.  
 
==PAGE:20==
 
20 
 
 
Figure 5 – A illustrative model for the ubiquitin-proteasome pathway 89 
 
 
2.4.2 Lysosomal proteolysis 
 
This pathway involves the degradation of proteins in the lysosome. Lysosomes are a type of organelle, 
containing different digestive enzymes, such as proteases, capable of degradation proteins. The lysosomal 
proteolysis is able to digest extracellular enzymes taken up by endocytosis (the uptake of extracellular 
material in vesicles formed from the plasma membrane), as well as cytoplasmic organelles and cytosolic 
proteins.  
Proteins are taken up by the lysosomes by different mechanism, one of them is macroautophagy, generally 
referred to as autophagy. In autophagy, proteins or organelles are taken up by membranes derived from the 
ER and forms a vesicle called an autophagosome. Then the autophagosome fuses with the lysosomes and 
forms a phagolysosome, in which the content is digested. Chaperones helps to unfold the proteins in the 
process. 
==PAGE:21==
 
21 
 
 
Figure 6 – A mitochondria taken up by – Proteins are degraded in the same way.  
 
The uptake of proteins into autophagosomes can both be nonselective and selective. The nonselective 
mechanism ensures that long-lived cytoplasmic proteins eventually are degraded, in order to ensure that no 
non-recognized damages proteins resides in the cell. And the selective pathway recognizes specific amino 
acid sequences, to target them to the lysosome. 
 
 
2.5 Intrinsically disordered proteins 
Usually proteins have been divided into 3 different classes based on three-dimensional structure and 
solubility, which are globular, membrane and fibrous proteins 90. Lately, a 4’th class of proteins has been 
discovered which are intrinsically disordered proteins.  
 
2.5.1 The 4 protein classes based on structure and solubility 
Globular proteins are the most abundant type of proteins in humans79. They are very soluble in aqueous 
solution and have a nearly spherical structure. They have a large amount of possible secondary structures 
and local folds. However, the amount of possible domains folds seems finite. These finite number of domain 
folds can be arranged in many ways, to construct different proteins with diverse functions 79. Globular 
proteins have functions including short- and long range transportation of molecules, catalyzation and 
regulation91. Examples are albumins, hemoglobin and super oxide dismutase. 
Membrane proteins are located in the plasma membrane (or in the Golgi or ER membrane) and often have 
extra- and/or intracellular domains. Membrane proteins interacts with the lipid bilayer, through hydrophobic 
amino acids on the protein surface, and can interacts with other membrane molecules, e.g. other proteins, 
through hydrogen bonding and ionic interactions 92. Membrane proteins are not soluble in aqueous 
==PAGE:22==
 
22 
 
environment and are for example involved in cell signaling and transportation through the membrane. 
Examples include the estrogen transporter, potassium channel and PSEN1. 
Fibrous proteins have a rod like structure and their main role in humans is to provide structure and support, 
for example in connective tissues, muscle fibers and bones 93. Fibrous proteins are made of elongated 
polypeptide chains running parallel to another and are stabilized by cross linkages such as di-sulfide bonds 
79. They are often not soluble in water. Examples are Actin and collagen. Collagen makes of about 30% of the 
total protein in the body, and has a coiled triple-helix structure 
 
Figure 7 – Triple helix chemical structure of collagen  79. PDB: 4GYX  
 
Intrinsically ordered proteins are proteins that lack a well-defined 3D structure in solution. For example they 
are not confined to the well-known regions of dihedral equilibrium positions in the Ramachandran plot, seen 
by ordered proteins 82. Above 1/3 of the crystal structures in the protein data bank have disordered regions 
of >50 consecutive residues 94. A well-used definition of a IDP is of having at least one disordered region of  
>50 consecutive residues 82 95. Both Prion and Tau are IPD’s according to this definition.  
A very well-known protein functions concept is the sequence
structure
function
→
→
, which until the early 
1990s was the nearly only accepted concept of protein function82. It states that the protein sequence 
determines a proteins unique structure, which most often is the global energy minimum of the hole protein. 
And this unique structure determines the unique function of the protein. That also means, when a protein 
loses its function, it because of a loss of the specific 3D structure 96. This concepts is thought to hold for nearly 
all globular, fibrous and membrane proteins 82. But the discovery of examples of IPD’s led to counterexamples 
of this 97,98, and the discovery of a whole new class of proteins, with many different conformations and 
functions depending on their binding partners 99. IPD’s undergo disorder-to-order transitions when binding 
to their binding partners 99. Therefore, almost all the folds that IPD’s undergoes, is not their most stable 
thermodynamically state.  
During the the disorder-to-order transition upon binding to binding partners, entropy is lost due to folding, 
but is compensated by an increase in binding enthalpy. 95 
IDP’s are depleted in hydrophobic amino acids and rich in hydrophilic (polar and charged) residues, making 
them more stable as a random coil in aqueous environment, and less prone to folding into a hydrophobic 
core and create specific tertiary structures 82.  
IDP’s are often less conserved than more structured proteins, due to the general trend in protein evolution 
that disordered regions exposed to water, are less conserved than buried amino acids 100.  
Phosporylation often induce folding in idp’s 99. 
IDP’s are often involved in signaling, regulation and control pathways, for example in molecular recognition 
of voltage gated ion channels, 101.  Crystal-structures are not suited analyze the unstructured states, but NMR 
can be used to characterize the structural propensities and dynamics of IDP’s 95. 
==PAGE:23==
 
23 
 
As IPD’s do not have specific 3D structures, IDP’s has no preference for chaperone binding 102.

3.1.2 Function(s) of PSEN1, gamma-secretase and APP 
 -secretase is an intramembrane endoprotease enzyme, that catalyzes the hydrolysis of certain integral 
membrane proteins such as Notch receptors, amyloid-beta precursor protein and other type I 
transmembrane receptors 106.  These substrates indicate that  -secretase normally functions as a moderator 
of signaling pathways 106. 
Presinilin-1 functions as the catalytic subunit in the membrane protein complex called  -secretase. Protease 
enzymes catalyzes the breakdown of proteins into smaller polypeptides or amino acids. The uncatalyzed 
hydrolysis of peptide bonds is extremely slow, taking hundreds of years.  Endo refers to the breakdown of 
==PAGE:27==
 
27 
 
peptides bonds from nonterminal amino acids. Other functions of presenilin-1 has been proposed, both 
within and outside of the gamma-secretase complex, such as involvement in protein trafficking and turnover, 
in calcium homeostasis and in regulation of beta-catenin signaling 107.  
APP has a high expression in neurons 105, where the protein is found in both somata, dendrites and axon. 
Evidence shows that APP and A s
 have an essential synaptic function, but the underlying mechanism 
remains unclear and controversial 105. The hypothesis’s is that APP and it’s A s
 is involved in cell-cell-, cell-
matrix- and synaptic-adhesion, in intracellular signaling, in apoptosis of neurons, in axon pruning and 
degeneration, in neural plasticity (the ability of neurons to change in form and function in response to 
alterations in their environment 108), in antimicrobial protection (by entrapment and neutralization of 
invading pathogens by A s
 109) and in metal homeostasis in the synaptic cleft. But it is not known whether 
APP and A s
 is a ligand or a receptor, a signaling molecule or plays a secondary role in gene regulation, and 
the coordination of function between APP and the different A s
 and the many intracellular binding partners 
remains unknown 105. 
Another substrate for gamma-secretase is the notch receptor protein which resides in the cell membrane. 
After cleave by alpha- and gamma-secretase, the Notch intracellular domain (NICD) is released, and travels 
to the cell nucleus to assemble into a transcription factor complex, to activate expression of certain genes 
110. These genes are involved in cell-fate determination during development and neurogenesis (the 
production of neurons from neural stem cells) 111. Abnormalities in Notch signaling is associated with defects 
in development and in several types of cancer.  
 
3.1.3 Catalytical mechanism 
More specifically, presenilin is a aspartyl protease with two catalytic ASP residues, ASP257 and ASP385 103. 
An aspartyl protease uses two aspartic acid residues to catalyze the hydrolysis of a peptide bond 112. Different 
mechanisms has been proposed 113, but the most accepted one is described by 112, and shown in Figure 12 
 
 
Figure 12 – mechanism of the catalyzed hydrolysis of a peptide bond by two aspartic molecules. Figure modified from 
https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aspartic_protease 
The pH makes only one of the aspartic acid residues unprotonated 112. The negatively charged aspartic acid 
residue attracts the hydrogen of the water molecule, to active the water molecule for a nucleophilic attack 
on the carbonyl carbon of the peptide bond. Additionally, the other aspartic acid residue helps to polarize 
the carbonyl group making it more electrophilic towards the incoming nucleophilic water 
113.  
This yields an oxyanion tetrahedral intermediate. Then the amide atom is protonated by one of the aspartic 
==PAGE:28==
 
28 
 
acid residues and a rearrangement leads to the breakdown of the intermediate to the two hydrolysis 
products 113.

3.2.1 Structure of SOD1 
 
 
Figure 14 – A structure of SOD1.  
 
The mature SOD1 are a homodimer, consisting of two identical SOD1 subunits bounded by a disulfide bond. 
The disulfide bond also stabilizes each subunit fold.  Each subunit consists of 8 antiparallel beta-strands, 
which form a beta-barrel 116.   
Each subunit contains one Cu and one Zn metal ion, both positioned outside the beta-barrel. The active site 
in each subunit contains of Cu coordinated to 3 histidine ligands, and a Zn ion coordinated to one aspartic 
acid and 3 histidine ligands. In the oxidized state of Cu, i.e. Cu(II), one of the histidine ligands is coordinated 
to both the Cu and the Zn metal ion116. This histidine is called the bridging ligand and have a white color in 
Figure 14.  
The metal ions both provide structural stability, and catalytical roles. The structure including the metal ions, 
gives the enzyme a suitable proton source to alter the metal ion redox potential suitable to superoxide redox 
reaction. Due to steric selection, larger anions such as phosphate are not able to pass into the active site.  
SOD1 is one of three human SOD isoforms. The other two SOD isoforms are SOD2 and SOD3, having bound 
Mn and Cu/Zn respectively in different sites. SOD2 and SOD3 catalyze the same chemical reaction as SOD1.  
==PAGE:30==

to Cu(II). Then Cu(II) is reduced to Cu(I) and superoxide is oxidized to molecular dioxygen, and the bond 
between Cu(II) and the bridging histidine is broken, in which the histidine gets protonated116.

3.2.3 SOD1 in relation to reactive oxygen species 
 
SOD1 is expressed in all types of cells, and makes up to 1-2% of the total soluble protein in the CNS 117. SOD1 
is highly conserved throughout evolution, for example human SOD1 shares 67 identity and 90% similarity 
over the 151 residues with “Alvinella pompejana” which is a deep-sea hydrothermal-vent worm, i.e. not a 
mammal 116.  
Superoxide is a molecule under the category of ROS. ROS is molecules and free radicals derived from 
molecular oxygen 118. Most of the ROS are produced as a biproduct during mitochondrial aerobic respiration. 
Superoxide is also produced in oxidative phosphorylation, photosynthesis and as intermediates of metal 
catalyzed oxidation reactions. Furthermore, some ROS, for example superoxide and hydrogen peroxide is 
important signaling molecules in the body, for example to regulate apoptosis, gene expression and the 
activation of cell signaling cascades 119. Moreover, superoxide is catalyzed from molecular oxygen by the 
NADPH oxidase enzyme in immune cells to help killing foreign bacteria in the human body 118. 
As the name states, ROS is very reactive. Atomic oxygen has two unpaired electrons in different orbitals in 
its outer electron shell, which is quite unstable. This electron configuration makes oxygen prone to accepting 
electrons, and formation of radicals and other ROS. ROS includes superoxide, peroxide, hydrogen peroxide, 
hydroxyl radical, hydroxyl ion. 
A tight regulation of superoxide is needed, because the high reactivity of superoxide makes it react with vital 
components of the cell such as DNA, proteins and other biomolecules. Cellular damage from ROS is involved 
in human diseases such as cardiovascular disease, cancer, neurodegenerative disease as well as aging 23. 
An imbalance towards a higher amount of ROS is called oxidative stress. All aerobic life forms need to have 
defense mechanism against ROS such as SOD1. Peroxisome (organelles in eukaryotic cells) converts the 
==PAGE:31==
 
31 
 
hydrogen peroxide formed by SOD1, into water and oxygen118, by the enzyme catalase.  
Cells having a high amount of metabolic activity such as liver and gill cells, have a higher amount of ROS 
formation, and therefore a higher expression level of antioxidant enzymes such as SOD1. 120.  
 
3.3 Parkin 
Parkin is an E3 ligase, that ubiquitinates protein substrate for degradation by the proteasome. Parkin is 
involved in other biological processes as well. 121 
3.3.1 Structure of Parkin 
Parkin consist of 6 domains. An Ub-like domain (UBL), a Linker domain, a RING0, RING1, and RING2 domain, 
an in-between-RING (IBR) domain and a repressor element (REP) domain. 121 . This is shown in the figures 
below.

Parkin is located in the cytosol.  
 
3.4 Prion 
Prion is an IPDs with many binding partners, and have been associated to many different functions, although 
current literature pictures the function of PrP as “unknown” or “uncertain” 126. Some assumed functions are 
protection against ER and oxidative stress126. It is expressed in most neural cell types, but mostly found in 
neurons and glial cells 127. 
 
3.4.1 Structure of Prion 
Mature prion consists of residue 23-231, while residue 1-22 is a signal peptide removed by PTM. The N-
terminal part contain functional subdomains, that can coordinate divalent metal ions, such as Zn and Cu. This 
==PAGE:33==
 
33 
 
flexibly disordered “tail” of residues 23-116 are not resolved but are shown as dots in Figure 19. A part of the 
unstructured tail is the octarepeat domain consisting of the repeated 8-residue sequence PHGGGWGQ. 
Humans have 4 of these repeats, and most species have 4 or 5 repeats 128. This octapeptide repeats 
contributes to the flexibility and is highly conserved.  
 
 
 
Figure 18 – Domains of the primary structure of Prion 
 
The mature PrP molecule can go through 3 different cleavages, for example alpha-cleavage as shown in Figure 
18, separating the C1 and N1 part. These 3 different cleavage events have unknown and discussed functions.  
Between the N- and C-terminal domains, resides a 20-residue domain of hydrophobic residues. This domain 
is located in the transmembrane area. The C-domain is a globular structure with 3 alpha helixes and 2 very 
short beta antiparallel beta strands (beta1 and beta2). The structure of the globular domain, is seen in Figure 
19 
 
Figure 19 – Structure of the globular domain of Prion. A flexible tail of unsolved structure is included as well. Figure from 127 
 
The octarepeat domain are highly selective for Cu2+. PrP has 4 coordinated copper ions. Upon binding of 
copper(II) the intrinsically disordered region, becomes more ordered128.  The globular domain has been 
  
  
  
  
  
  
==PAGE:34==
 
34 
 
reported to be important for import of PrP into the ER129. The N-terminus flexible tail are important for 
binding to binding partners.  
The prion protein is soluble in the cell and exist as a monomer. PrP is anchored to the outside of the plasma 
membranes and is therefore located mainly in the extracellular space, where it interacts with different 
molecular partners.  A disulfide bond is present between alpha2 and alpha3 to provide structural stability.  
After syntheses in the ER, the signal peptide directs the protein to the Golgi, where N-linked glycans attaches, 
and the protein is sorted for trafficking to the cell surface. On the cell surface, the C-terminal is attached to 
the GPI anchor, mainly in lipid rafts.  
 
3.4.2 Function(s) of Prion 
The intrinsically disordered N-terminal has many binding partners. One of the most discussed functions is its 
involvement in cell signaling. With the extracellular location, PrP could be including in cellular signaling by 
receiving messages from the extracellular environment and transmit the signal into the cell with the help of 
molecular binding partners in the cell membrane, for example with the neural cell adhesion molecule 
NCAM130. It is suggested that these two together can promote neurogenesis (formation of new neutrites 
which can develop into axons and dendrites). 
 
Functions of Prion has been proposed, both on the molecular, cellular and systemic level, as seen in Table 5 
131–133.

PrP might protect cells from oxidative stress by regulating antioxidant enzymes such as SOD1 by 
incorporating copper into SOD1 and thereby regulating their activity. Furthermore ROS may induce beta-

3.5 Tau 
As a MAP 134, tau helps to assemble tubules into microtubules and to stabilize microtubules135. Tau is an 
IDP136, 
and 
therefore 
have 
possible 
other 
roles, 
such 
as 
neurite 
outgrowth 
135. 
Tau may exist in a protein family of microtubule associated proteins, including tau MAP2 and MAP4 proteins  
 
3.5.1 Structure of tau 
 
The primary structure of tau can be divided into at least four domains: A N-terminal domain, a proline-rich 
domain, a microtubule binding domain (MTBR) and a carboxy terminus 137, as shown in figure Figure 20. 
 
Figure 20 – Schematic overview of tau domains according to 137 
The carboxyl terminal domain can inhibit tau filament formation. The N-terminal domain have presumed 
roles in determining the spacing between microtubles138, anchoring to the membrane 139 and dimerization of 
tau140 .The proline-rich region is meant to enhance binding and assembly of the microtubule 141.  
The microtubule binding domain are important for binding of microtubules and contains 3 or 4 binding 
regions, containing conserved regions. Cystine residues at the MTBR domain can be involved in tau forming 
multimers to other tau proteins.  
There are mainly six isoforms in the CNS, with differences in the primary structure, all originated from the 
Mapt gene, by alternate splicing 134. The longest isoform is shown in Figure 20. 
Tau is heat-stable and soluble in acid, which is unusual properties of animal proteins 142. 
In solution, tau has a high flexibility and is characterized as an intrinsic disordered protein (IDP) 134. I.e. it lacks 
secondary structure and behaves like a random coil. Therefore, tau can form many folded states, and is not 
suitable to be studied by crystallographic methods 134, but can be studied by spectroscopic methods such as 
NMR.  
==PAGE:36==
 
36 
 
2 of the many conformations of tau are shown in Figure 21. The extended fibrillar form is an unfolded form 
in which no secondary or tertiary structure is present 143. This form can transform into a paperclip form in 
which  the C-terminus folds over the MTBR domain and then the N-terminal folds over the C-terminal 137.  
 
Figure 21 – Figure of extended and paperclip conformation of tau 137 
 
Tau can form dimers oligomers and larger polymers with other tau molecules, known as aggregates or fibers.   
When binding to microtubule, tau cannot form interactions to other tau molecules 
134.  
A tertiary structure form of the tau protein is also suggested as one of its conformation, upon binding to the 
antibody Alz50, because both the C- and N-terminus reacts with the antibody 144. 
Tau is intrinsically disordered in solution and makes only a little increase in order upon binding, making it 
hard to resolve the structure 136. 
 
 
3.5.2 Function of tau 
The main function of tau is thought of as an essential factor to assemble, stabilize and regulate the growth  
of microtubules145, like other MAP’s. Tau has been reported to bind to MT’s both at the outer146 and inner147 
surface of MT’s . And to bind to tubulin’s negatively charged C-tail as well as in between the α/β-tubulin 
subunits145.  
Tau is highly soluble in aqueous solution, due to many charges, and can interaction with >30 partners in the 
cytosol 148.  Once bound to its binding partner, tau gets more rigid and can perform its function, like other 
IDP’s. Other than microtubules, tau can for example bind to neurofilaments, actin filaments, ribosomes and 
ubiquitin 148.  
Tau can bind to the minor groove of DNA, thereby hindering its metabolism. This suggest a role of tau in the 
stabilization of DNA. 137. 
==PAGE:37==
 
37 
 
The C-terminus can bind to axonal microtubules and the N-terminus can bind to components of the neural 
plasma membrane 149,150. This suggest a role of stabilizing the membrane and/or to act as a linking molecule 
between microtubules and the plasma membrane  137. 
 
3.5.3 Location and regulation 
Tau is mainly located in the brain in neurons 148, primarily in axons in the neurons, and to a lesser extend in 
dendrites at the soma, in glial cells and in the cell nucleus 151 152.  
Tau has many phosphorylation sites. The given phosphorylation state is regulated in order to determine the 
function of tau, to determine the specific binding partners such as microtubules and nucleic acids, and to 
define the location of tau 134. This process is reversible, because protein phosphatases can cleave off the 
phosphorylation, when the cell is done using tau to that function it had with the given phosphorylation 137. 
Phosphorylation of tau can make tau more rigid 153 and make tau obtain secondary structures such as alpha-
helix or beta-sheets 134. 
 
4 Disease mechanisms of NNDs including destability of proteins 
 
The hypothesis in this project, that neurodegenerative diseases are due to destabilized native proteins, fits 
well with most of the cellular and clinical aspects seen in NDDs.   
In general, destabilized proteins, can lead to disease in two overall ways: 
1: The destabilized proteins, cannot perform their proper function, leading to disease.  
2: The destabilized proteins, leads to a gain of toxic function.  
Below, some of the most hot and controversial disease hypotheses for NDDs are briefly explained, and how 
destabilized proteins fit into their explanation.   
 
Protein aggregation 
Aggregation of proteins and neurodegenerative diseases are undoubtedly associated, because aggregates 
are seen in all of the neurodegenerative diseases4. But the mechanism between these two, are highly 
unknown. Usually the aggregates were thought to be toxic to the neurons and brain, because they take up 
tissue space and break neuronal connections, and thereby cause neurodegenerative diseases. 
Proteins are highly optimized through evolution, and mutations most often destabilize the protein. If it is true 
that pathogenic mutations are more destabilizing than mutations in non-conserved regions, then the global 
minimum of the native state on Figure 4 are shifted to a much higher in energy, making more proteins going 
into the aggregation state. These aggregates acts as seeds to further aggregations of the otherwise proper 
folded proteins, and eventually almost no proteins are left in the native state. Thereby, the protein cannot 
perform its regular function, resulting in disease. Furthermore, these proteins misfolded due to mutations or 
oxidative stress, can cause other types of proteins to aggregate, and suddenly many different proteins have 
==PAGE:38==
 
38 
 
lost their proper function. I.e. destabilization of proteins is the main cause of NDDs, and aggregations is a 
side effect. This can also explain the complexity of the NDDs.  
Otherwise, the destabilization of proteins leading to favorable protein aggregates, could lead to a gain of 
toxic function of the protein aggregates. However, aggregate inhibitor drugs have failed to benefit the 
patients. 
An example of this, is CJD and prion disease. The most widely accepted hypothesis behind the mechanism of 
Creutzfeldt Jacob disease and other prion diseases, comes from the evidence that when misfolded prion, 
PrPSc, are injected into wild-type animals, they get Prion diseases including CJD. Furthermore, knock-out mice 
are resistant to prion infection. This states that the misfolded PrPSc, converts the natively folded prion protein 
PrPc into its misfolded form. Then the prion protein is not in its ordinary cellular form, PrPC, and therefore 
cannot performs its ordinary function, which causes disease. Otherwise the aggregates are toxic. I.e. the 
disease mechanism is either a loss of function of PrPC and unfolded PrPC or toxic aggregates. 
Furthermore, in sporadic cjd, no mutations are found in the Prion protein. But oxidative stress could 
destabilize PrPC, leading to PrPSc and therefore non-function of PrPC. Protein aggregates of PrPSc can makes 
other proteins aggregate and cause a loss of function of different proteins.  
 
Protein oligomerization 
The failure of aggregate inhibiting drugs, the fact that aggregates also are present in some healthy people, 
and the fact that neurons with aggregates seem healthier than nearby neurons without aggregates in 
patients with AD and PD, have countered the hypothesis of the aggregates being toxic. Instead, todays most 
accepted hypothesis is that the process of protein misfolding and the oligomers are the real culprits 4.  
This fits well with the destabilizing hypothesis of this project. Because when the native form of the protein is 
destabilized due to genetic mutations or oxidative stress through aging, the native form is disfavored, in 
which the equilibrium is shifted towards the oligomer forms. This leads to no stable native protein forms, i.e. 
the protein cannot form its proper ordinary function. A side effect of this is protein oligomerization. 
Otherwise protein oligomers are toxic, but still causes by destabilized proteins due to mutations or oxidative 
stress. Although, oligomer inhibitor drugs have failed to benefit the patients.  
 
Energy depletion 
When proteins are misfolded, they are usually detected by the protein quality control system, and either 
refolded into the native state with the help of chaperones or degraded154. This protein homeostasis is 
referred to as proteostasis. This causes a lot of energy to be used to protein turnover, leading to an 
insufficient amount of energy left for normal signaling in the CNS, leading to the NDDs 155.  
If this energy depletion is the true disease mechanism in NDDs, then destabilization of proteins due to 
pathogenic mutations or oxidative stress leading to misfolded proteins, could likely be the true reason for 
this energy depletion.

39

allintitle: (prion OR prnp OR PrP) + ("Creutzfeldt‐Jakob disease" OR cjd) + (mutation OR mutations OR 
polymorphism OR polymorphisms OR missense OR novel OR variant OR variants) 
allintitle: (presenilin-1 OR PSEN1 OR PS1) + (mutation OR mutations OR polymorphism OR polymorphisms

Note that the parenthesis can be omitted but are present for clarification.  
For Prion and Tau, the searches include the disease as well, because for these two proteins, a lot of results 
from other diseases came up as well. For the other 3 proteins, the relevant diseases almost only showed up 
in the search output.  
Only mutations in which an individual has been confirmed to have the particular disease, were used.  
Age of onset data of the variants was notated when available. Age of onset data also makes the pathogenicity 
of variant more reliable. When age of onset data was available from different families and/or from different 
sources, a weighted average of the age of onset was calculated based upon the number of patients carrying 
the mutation.   
Unfortunately, the well-known “Alzheimer Disease & Frontotemporal Dementia Mutation Database” and 
“Parkinson disease Mutation database” hosted by “VIB Center for Molecular Neurology” was out of order by 
the time of this thesis. However, the supervisor Kasper Kepp provided printed copies of these databases for 
PSEN1 and Parkin from 2015, which was used.

These 15 PDB-structures has been selected based upon 3 factors. The most important factor was the 
number of missing residues residing in familial mutant residues. This number has to be as low as possible, 
in order to get as many clinical mutations as possible included in the analyses. The second factor was the 
resolution, which needs to be low as possible in order to get an accurate protein structure. The last factor 
was the completeness of the mutation-residues. I.e. the third structure was preferred over another one, if 
it included residues that were missing in the two other structures. Furthermore, no structures were 
selected with a resolution higher than 4.5Å.  
Some of the pdb-structures have mutated residues. Pymol have been used to mutate the residue back to 
the wildtype. Different rotamer configurations of the residue is possible, and the one that gives the least 
strain has been selected.   
 
5.1.3 Control dataset 
The control dataset will be used to compare the clinical mutations, to other mutations, in order to study 
whether a statistically significant difference in mean value exist between ΔΔG values of the clinical 
mutations and other mutations.  
The control data set can be created in (at least) 3 different ways.  
1: Using ALL possible mutations 
2: Using mutations from clinically confirmed non-pathogenic mutations 
3: Using mutations from non-conserved residues. 
The first method uses all possible mutations. This has been the current method to use but has a big 
disadvantage because this also includes mutations in conserved sites that would never happen in nature, 
because such mutations would destroy the protein and its function. Such mutations are too pathogenic, 
and will not be carried on through evolution, because then the proteins either cannot fold probably, or 
loses its function in the active site. Furthermore, this dataset will also include the pathogenic mutations 
from the pathogenic dataset. However, it would be possible to exclude the pathogenic mutations. 
The second method is to use clinically confirmed non-pathogenic mutations. This will create the most 
reliable control data set, because then it would be easy and clear to compare the ΔΔG values between 
pathogenic and non-pathogenic mutations and investigate the difference between those two.  
But very few clinically confirmed non-pathogenic mutations are available in the literature, so the control set 
will be too small to see statistically significant differences between pathogenic and non-pathogenic 
mutations.  
==PAGE:43==
 
43 
 
The last method is the one that have been used. This has a big advantage comparted to method one, 
because only mutations in non-conserved residues is used, i.e. excluding mutations that would perform a 
completely dysfunctional protein. However, a disadvantage is that unknown pathogenic mutations could be 
present in this dataset. 
Previously, control data sets have mostly been created by method 1, so a brainstorming has been 
performed in order to come up with ideas to create a control set by method 3. After the brainstorming, two 
reliable methods for creating the non-conserved control dataset was obtained. These are: 
1.  To use NCBI’s Conserved Domain Database (CDD).  
This database consists of a collection of well-annotated multiple sequence alignment models for ancient 
domains and full-length proteins 156. The protein sequence can be entered, and then the webservers 
performs multiple sequence alignment in the CDD database, in order to find known protein sequences from 
NCBI, that are similar to the query sequence. A threshold for information content can be set, and then each 
site can be clarified as conserved or no conserved (information content is the degree of conserveness).  
This method has two main disadvantages. Firstly, the webserver uses the most similar protein sequences in 
the database, and it is not possible to manually select the organisms. Therefore, a different set of 
organisms is used for each protein, i.e. a different evolutionary distance for each protein is used. This is bad 
because then the conserveness of the control dataset will not only depend on how conserved the proteins 
are but will also depend on which organisms that are used.  
Secondly, not only “animals” are included, but all life such as plants and bacteria are included as well. 
Therefore, the evolutionary distance is very far from the human proteins in some of the alignments. 
Moreover, NDDs are not seen in plants and bacteria.  
 
2.  To use Uniprot to find common annotations and align sequences 
This is the methods that has been used.  
A search has been performed, in order to find those animals, in which Uniprot-annotations exists for all 5 
proteins. This ensures that the same evolutionary distance is used for all 5 alignments. A total of 26 animals 
was found in Uniprot, in which sequences exist for all 5 proteins. These 26 animals are seen in Table 12 
Organism 
Whether the animal is present in both the “more” and “less” 
conserved alignment or only in the less conserved alignment.

A multiple alignment between the 26 sequences was then performed for each protein, by the Uniprot 
website. Uniprot uses Clustal Omega as the alignment program. An example of an output of the alignment 
from superoxide dismutate are shown in Figure 22.  
 
Figure 22 – Multiple sequence alignment of superoxide dismutase from the 26 animals in Table 12.  
In the alignment output, fully conserved residues are marked with an * (asterisk). A : (colon) indicates 
conservation between groups of strongly similar properties, i.e. scoring > 0.5 in PAM 250 matrix. And 
resiudes with a . (period) indicates conservation between groups of weakly similar properties, i.e. scoring 
=< 0.5 in the PAM 250 matrix 157 . When a gap is present, the reside are not marked with a symbol.  A PAM 
matrix is used to determine the similarity of aligned sequences. 
Then all the alignments are saved to MATLAB. A conserved residue is then defined as a position, with only 
one amino acid present at that position. 2 or more possible amino acids is then a non-conserved residue. A 
script has been written, in order to perform that task, and is given in Appendix C and called 
“counting_res_alignment.m” . The final output of this script is a vector of yes/no for each position, that tells 
whether the residue is conserved or non-conserved. Then the non-conserved residues are extracted. Note 
that gaps are counted as a conserved residue. This is very unusual because gaps usual exist in very non-
conserved regions in nature, and insertions and deletions are usually more extreme alterations in the 
peptide sequence than point mutations, indicating a non-conserved segment. But many of the used protein 
sequences seem to include gaps that are not true deletions or insertions seen in nature, but rather a lack of 
true residues for the true protein sequence, i.e. an error in the protein-annotation. Furthermore, it is only 
missense point mutations that are the subject of this project. An example is seen in Figure 23 in which the 
==PAGE:45==
 
45 
 
TR|G1PIA9|G1P1A9_MYOLU entry includes a gap, but the other 25 protein sequences clearly show 
conserved residues.  
 
Figure 23 – A gap in SOD1 for all animals.  
 
In order to investigate the dependence of the degree of conserveness, another alignment was performed 
with fewer animals, that creates a more conserved alignment. All animals with an “Unitprot annotation 
score” between 3 and 5 was selected, which resulted in 17 animals. The annotation score, provides a 
heuristic measure of the annotation content of a UniProtKB entry or proteome 158. Therefore, this dataset 
might also contain more correct annotations, however as stated by UniProt, “the annotation score cannot 
be used as a measure of the accuracy of the annotation as we cannot define the ‘correct annotation’ for 
any given protein.  
The alignment including 17 animals are called the “more conserved alignment”, and the one including 26 
animals are called the “less conserved alignment”. 
A third alignment including only the primates of the original 26 alignments is also performed. The primates 
are marked with a * in Table 12. As most NNDs only are seen in primates, this makes sense. For PS-1 and 
Tau, the alignments with primates yielded only 1 non-conserved sites. Such a control data set is too small to 
give a large critical mass, so the analysis for these two proteins with the primate control are not conducted.    
The following figure shows a plot of the number of conserved residues in each protein: 
==PAGE:46==
 
46 
 
 
Figure 24 – Percent of conserved residues in the 3 types of control sets.   
 
The globular C-terminal region of Prion are reported in the literature to be highly conserved 159. 
Investigation of the C-terminal in the alignment shows that 22 of the sequences includes almost the same 
residues, but 4 of the alignments are very off in some regions. These are chicken,  Ma's night monkey, 
Zebra finch and Chinese alligator. This might be the truth that these animals have big variations compared 
to the other ones, but this could also be due to either a bad alignment or mistakes in the annotation. For 
that reason, the analysis is made without these 4 animals as well.   
Similarly, for Parkin 4 sequence alignments are seen to derivative a lot from the ones, so an analysis with 
these removed are tried.   
 
5.1.4 Calculation of ΔΔG values 
A lot of ΔΔG values is needed. The 3 ΔΔG methods all have different interfaces, so the method varies and 
will be explained in the following sections.   
For multimers only mutations were made in the first chain including the protein.  
 
FoldX 
For FoldX, the Ubuntu version has been used, on VirtualBox. The packages have been downloaded from 
http://foldxsuite.crg.eu/. This creates a folder, that has been put into VirtualBox. The program has been 
run, by running the following command line for the Prion protein with pdb: 3hak.pdb.  
“foldx --command=BuildModel --pdb=3hak.pdb --mutant-file=individual_list_3hak.txt“
