As seen, the program needs the pdb structure for the protein, and then a mutation file which includes a list 
for mutations. This command is an example for the clinical mutations for the pdb-structure “3hak.pdb”. The 
mutant-file is shown in Figure 25. 
 
Figure 25: mutation file for Prion with pdb structure 3hak. Name of file: individual_list_3hak.txt 
 
Each line includes each mutation. Column 1 is the wild type amino acid. Column 2 is the chain in the pdb-
structure. The next column is the position for the mutation. And the last column is the mutated amino acid. 
Several files then come as output, and one of them includes the ΔΔG values in kcal/mol.  
The mutant file has been created by copying the wild type the positions and the mutated residue from the 
excel. In that way, no typing errors is made.  
For further users, it is suggested to compute all 19 mutations in all residues for once, and then extract the 
needed mutations for the given control sets.  
 
Popmusic 
The ΔΔG calculations in popmusic, has been perfomed in the webserver. Popmusic has a useful tool 
(compared to the other programs), in that it can perform all possible mutations in less than a minute. This 
is called “systematic” on the popmusic webserver.  
==PAGE:48==
 
48 
 
However only a fraction of all the mutations has been used. For the clinical mutation dataset, the 
mutations have been manually extracted from the systematic file and has been double checked. 
For the control set, more than 2000 mutations need to be perfomed. Therefore, in order to minimize the 
time and to minimize human errors, a script has been created, in order to extract those ΔΔG values that are 
needed. The script can be seen in Error! Reference source not found. and are called “Popmusic.m”.  
 
Imutant 
The Imutant program has been used by Ubuntu on VirtualBox. The following command is used to run the 
program: 
Python I-Mutant3.0.py -pdb 1cei.pdb 1cei.dssp A 17 ALL 
“A” is the chain. “17” is the residue number. And “ALL” makes the program calculate all 19 mutations for 
the given residue.  
As seen, Imutant offers to calculate all mutations for each residue at a time. Each residue has been inserted 
manually, and then the ΔΔG values for the 19 mutations has been copied. This requires that the correct 
mutations are typed each time. In order to ensure that no errors were made, the positions were copied and 
compared to the excel file in matlab, to check whether the correct positions were used.  
An improvement of this method would be to create a for loop in a Shell script, that automatically calculated 
the desired mutations for all the residues, based upon a file including the mutations.  
The Imutant program needs a dssp file as input. A dssp file includes an assignment of the secondary 
structure for each residue. The dssp file has can be calculated based on a 3D structure of the protein (the 
pdb-file). The webserver “https://www3.cmbi.umcn.nl/xssp/”has been used to calculate this dssp file, using 
the pdb-files as input.  
 
 
Notepad ++ 
Notepad++ has been used a lot to perform manipulations of data. Overall, notepad has been used to the 
following manipulations: 
- 
To switch between “,” and “’.”, as the Danish version of excel has been used, which uses “,” as a 
decimal separator.  
- 
To create mutation files.  
 
 
5.2 Methods for ΔΔG computation 
Point mutations changes the free energy of the protein. This free energy change is given by:  
mutant
WT
G
G
G

= 
−
 
==PAGE:49==
 
49 
 
I.e. the difference between the free energy of the mutant protein and the wild type.  
3 methods have been chosen for computing the ΔΔG values. These are FoldX, Popmusic and I-Mutant 3.0. 
These 3 methods are very different in the way they are constructed, which can give an interesting and reliable 
comparison. Furthermore they are reliable according to benchmarks 160–162, which shows good results on 
average.  
 
5.2.1 Stability of proteins in general and in disease 
A stabilizing protein point mutation is one having ΔΔG < 0 kcal/mol. 
A destabilizing protein point mutation is one having ΔΔG > 0 kcal/mol.  
However, usually mutations with a ΔΔG between 0.5 and -0.5 kcal/mol is classified as a neutral mutation, 
because the experimental error for the measurement of ΔΔG has been estimated to be 0.48
/
kcal mol

.  
Proteins are optimized to be stable under physiological conditions. The overall thermodynamic stability, i.e. 
ΔG of folding, is seen in the range -5 to -15 kcal/mol163 (the energy of a hydrogen bond is typically 2-5 
kcal/mol). Consequently, the effect of single point mutations could severely  alter the protein stability. 
Actually, some single point mutations are so severe, that they make the protein collapse and unfold, for 
example a substitution from a hydrophobic residue into a hydrophilic one, in the core of the protein 164. 
Despite that, some proteins seem to have many tolerable and stabilizing mutations165. An analysis of 
experimental ΔΔG for 1285 mutations from 10 different proteins from the Protherm database, are shown as 
the black curve in Figure 26. 
 
Figure 26 – ΔΔG of single point mutations from 5 different proteins, and for a experimental data set of 1285 mutations.  
The effects of point mutations in proteins, seems to be universally distributed, and follow a normal 
distribution163. The experimental computations show a mean value of ΔΔG=0.48 kcal/mol and the FoldX 
prediction on these show a mean value of ΔΔG=0.48. I.e. in general, most mutations are destabilizing to the 
protein structure. This reflects that proteins are highly optimized, and consequently it is hard to optimize a 
protein. The surface residues demonstrate a narrow distribution with a mildly destabilizing mean ΔΔG (∼0.6 
kcal/mol), whereas the core residues demonstrate a wider distribution with a stronger destabilizing mean 
(∼1.4 kcal/mol) 163.  
==PAGE:50==
 
50 
 
Destabilization of proteins can lead to reduction in hydrophobic area, overpacking, backbone strain, and loss 
of electrostatic interactions 166. An analysis of protein single point mutations, has showed that up to 80% 
cases of diseases, are due to destabilization of protein structure due to point mutations167. This was based 
on visual assessment of the effect of an amino acid substitution on the protein structure and function. 
Another result, based on a computational study shows that approximately half of the mutations randomly 
picked from a large data set of disease causing mutations, are correlated with decrease in stability 166. This is 
however biased towards that mutations in general are more detailing than stabilizing.  
 
 
5.2.2 ΔΔG computing methods 
The most reliable ΔΔG values come from the experimental determination of the change in folding free energy 
upon mutation. But this method is costly and time consuming and does not fit to explore several mutations 
168.  
As the evolution has created highly optimized proteins, single amino acid mutations can significantly change 
the stability of a protein structure 160. 4 classification can be made to distinguish between the methods of 
predicting protein stability changes resulting from single amino acid mutations: (1) physical potential energy 
functions approach (2) statistical potential energy functions, (3) empirical potential energy function and (4) 
machine learning methods 169.   
Of these 4, the physical potential energy functions (such as molecular mechanics approaches or Monte Carlo 
simulations) are most probably the most accurate methods, because they directly simulate the atomic force 
fields present in a given protein structure, but are too computationally intensive for each mutation 160. For 
that reason, only the 3 other methods will be used.  
Mostly support vector machines has been used as the machine learning tool (Fx for I-Mutant). 
 
 
5.2.3 Popmusic 
Popmusic uses statistical potential energy functions derived from statistical analysis of protein structures, 
using environmental trends, substitution frequencies and correlations of contacting residues, in solved 
protein 3-dimensional structures.

The statistical potentials are derived from a dataset of known protein structures, and reflects the preferences 
of different amino acids to adapt particular local conformations, to be more or less accessible to the solvent 
or to be separated by a given spatial distance 170. All these features contribute to the total free energy of the 
protein. 
Formally, the statistical potentials describe the correlations between various sequence or structure 
descriptors of the same amino acid or of neighboring ones 171. The descriptors are for each residue: the amino 
acid type si, the torsion angles defining the backbone conformation ti, the solvent accessibility ai, and for each 
pair of residues the spatial distance between the average geometric centers of their side chains, dij. In 
popmusic 2.1, 13 potentials are used, and they are denoted:

If the mutant amino acid is smaller, a cavity is created. And if the mutant amino acid is larger, this may induce 
stress in the structure. Both cases are likely to destabilize the protein 168. The statistical potentials cannot 
describe these effects correctly, because they are derived from a dataset of native structures of wild-type 
proteins, which are highly optimized through evolution, and therefore have very few packing defects 168.  
Increasing the number of mutants and proteins, does not improve the predictive power of the algorithm170. 
This is due to that the current simple potentials already capture almost all the correlation between the 
distributors and inputting new proteins does not come up with new correlations.

slope. wi are called the scaling factor (called weights in machine learning) and bi the vertical shift (called bias 
in machine learning).

To compute the 16 whitening coefficients, a neural network with one hidden sigmoid layer with sigmoid 
nodes has been used. We have a total of 64 different parameters to estimate (16 Ci, 16 ri, 16 wi and 16 bi), 
and we want to find the lowest value of sigma in eq, i.e. the parameters giving the smallest difference 
between the experimentally measured and the predicted folding free energy.

The data set used to train and validate the model, i.e. the experimentally measured folding free energy of 
mutant proteins, contains 2648 different point mutations in 131 proteins. These proteins were extracted 
from the ProTherm database, all having experimentally determined ΔΔG values. Mutations that destabilize 
the structure by more than 5 kcal/mol and mutations involving proline was removed, as they are likely to 
induce significant structural changes that are not modelled by PopMusiC. 168. The folding free energy was 
changed to be in ΔΔG per mole of monomer. To avoid redundancy of several measurements for the same 
mutant, an average was perfomed, with a higher weight to conditions near pH 7, temperature 25C and 
without additives. 
A 5-fold crossvalidaiton procedure was used, to train the neural network, i.e. to find the 64 parameters. In 
this, first the parameters of equation … was set to initial conditions, and then mathematical functions such 
as the Levenberg-Marquard algorithm was used to find the parameters that gives the lowest value of sigma, 
on 4/5 of the total protein set. Then the performance was evaluated on the remaining 1/5. And this was done 
5 times. The performance on the training set was only a little bit better than the performance on the 
validation set, indicated that the model has not been overfitted. 
When the ΔΔG value of a mutant in a PDB-structure is going to be estimated by the method, the method uses 
the pdb-structure of the wild-type protein, to calculate the statistical potentials for both the wild-type and 
the mutant protein, and then uses equation 1 to estimate the ΔΔG.  
 
It is most efficient to use different energy functions in the core and on the surface of the proteins, due to 
different interactions in different environments. For that reason, energy functions depending on the surface 
accessibility has been used.  
 
 
5.2.4 FoldX 
FoldX uses experimental experiments to create an energy function that describes the physical interactions in 
the proteins 173. 
The experimental protein mutant structures contain all kinds of secondary structures (turns, alpha and 310-
helix, beta-sheet, coil), contains both hydrophobic and polar residue mutations and mutations in different 
kind of solvent-accessibility. This ensures that the most important types of interactions that govern protein 
stability are present 173.  
The energy function in FoldX calculates the energy difference between the wild type and the mutant protein

solvH
G

 and 
solvP
G

 is the energy contribution from the interaction with the solvent. These solvent 
parameters are the difference in solvation energy for hydrophobic and polar groups, respectively, when going 
from the unfolded state to the folded state. These has been calculated from experiments in which the 
different amino acids have been transferred from water to organic solvents, which imitates the energy for 
an amino acid that is going from a solvent exposed unfolded state to a buried folded state during folding.  
In a similar way, the sum of van der Waals contributions 
vdw
G

 from all the atoms, has been calculated by 
experiments in which the amino acids have been transferred from water to vapor. This gives the sum of the 
solvation and van der Waals energies, and then the van der Waals energy is calculated by subtracting the 
already calculated solvation energy.   
The electrostatic contribution 
el
G

 from charged amino acids and the charged N and C terminal, is calculated 
from Coulomb’s law in which the dielectric constant is scaled with the extend of how much the bond is buried. 
The free energy contribution from the hydrogen bonds 
hbond
G

, is the difference in energy between an intra-
molecular and an inter-molecular hydrogen bond 173. This has been calculated by studying the geometrics of 
inter- and intra- hydrogen bonds and the energies of these.  
Water molecules that makes more than two hydrogen bonds to the protein and therefore has a persistent 
interaction with the protein (called a water bridge) has been calculated explicitly in the 
wb
G


G

 
is an extra stabilizing free energy provided by these fixed water molecules.

sc

locations, upon folding. 
kon
G


between the multiple polypeptides chains. 
Finally,

Similar results were obtained, showing that no significant bias was introduced from fitting the weights and 
the parameters to the training database. 
When the ΔΔG of a protein mutation is going to be calculated, the program calculates the free energy terms 
in for the WT and the mutant protein, based on the structure of the WT and on the pre-computed 
coefficients. And then ΔΔG can be calculated using equation xxx and the weights.

5.2.5 I-Mutant 3.0 
I-Mutant is a support vector method, that can take either a structure or a sequence as input and estimate 
both the sign and the actual ΔΔG value upon a point mutation. In I-Mutant the temperature and pH need to 
be specified, which was set to 25C and pH=7 in this thesis.  
In other word, SVM can both be used as classifiers (predicting the sign of ΔΔG) or regression (predicting the 
value of ΔΔG). It is easiest to introduce SVM as a classification task and extend this to the regression. 
In the classification task, we have two sets of classes. We want to find a plane, that separates two classes of 
data points, see figure Figure 27. In this case, the data are linearly separable. Later we introduce kernels, 
which is used when the data is not linearly separable. 
A lot of different planes, called hyperplanes, can separate these two data classes in the figure, but SVM finds 
the optimal one. The optimal hyperplane is the one that maximize the separation from the hyperplane, to 
the nearest data point of both classes. These nearest points are called support vectors. 
 
 
Figure 27 – Two margins and the maximum-margin hyperplane, for a SVM trained with samples from two classes. Link: 
https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Support-vector_machine#/media/File:SVM_margin.png 
A hyperplane can be written as the set of points x satisfying 
0
w x b
−=
, where w is a normal vector to the 
hyperplane. We want to find two parallel hyperplanes that separate the two classes of data, such that the 
distance between them is as large as possible. The maximum-margin hyperplane is the hyperplane that lies 
halfway between these two parallel planes. And the region that is bounded by these two parallel planes, is 
called the margin. 
When we want to classify into -1 and 1 (i.e. into destabilizing and stabilizing mutation) the two sets of points, 
need to have the constrains:

56

hyperplane nor on the solution.

mutation. 
The last input value is the relative solvent accessible area of the mutated residue. This is the accessible 
surface area calculated by the DSSP program, divided by the free residue surface 176.  
Each protein mutant xi has an associated yi value, which is either 1 or -1 for a destabilizing or stabilizing 
mutation respectively. 
 
For the SVM regression, we have a very similar optimization problem, given by:

Now yi is the experimental ΔΔG value for the protein mutant xi. These experimental ΔΔG values are derived 
from the ProTherm database, which currently is the most complete database of thermodynamically 
experimentally data of free energy changes of protein stability upon mutation under different conditions 177. 
 is a user defined value, which is the maximum error we want to have on our predictions. I.e. the method 
requires that all the predictions need to be within an  range from the experimentally determined values.

I.e. the final optimized model consists of i number of weights w, and one bias value b.

Kernel function,  
The problem above, is called a quadratic programming problem or a constrained optimization problem, and 
can be solved by a variety of algorithms, typically by the Lagrangian multiplier method, which is beyond the 
scope of this thesis. But as the function is quadratic, the solution surface is paraboloid, which has just a single 
global minimum.  
In the SVM classifier, the algorithm ignored training points that lied beyond the margin. In the SVM regression 
case, the algorithm ignores training points with an error below , and only optimizes the function to correctly 
classifying the training data with an error bigger than epsilon.  
When solving the optimization problem, most often data points cannot be fit with a linear line. I.e. there is 
no linear relationship between the structure of the protein mutants, and their associated 
G

 value. This 
is the case for the data points on (b) in Figure 28.  
 
Figure 28 – Regression using a Kernel function for SVM. Figure from 160 
Instead we can map the data into a higher dimensional feature space by a so called kernel function K(xi,xj) , 
and in this feature space, there exist a linear relationship, as seen on the right plot on (a) in Figure 28.  
When solving the optimization problem, we solve it in the feature space.  
When I-Mutant was created, four different Kernel function was tested: Linear, polynomial, Sigmoid and RBF. 
And it was found that the RBF yielded the most accurate predicitons 177. It is given by:

5.2.6 Accuracy of methods 
A lot of comparisons of the predictor methods has been performed. One reference161 which shows a accuracy 
of 0.64 and 0.54 on I-Mutant 3.0 and FoldX respectively. The accuracy is given by 
 Accuracy=
tp
tn
tp
tn
fp
fn
+
+
+
+
 
t for true, p for positive, n for negative and f for false. I.e. the accuracy measures the expertise of the methods 
for classifying protein mutations into being destabilizing or destabilizing. It does not evaluate the exact ΔΔG 
value.  
The accuracy measurement  is based on predicting whether a mutation is destabilizing or stabilizing  for 1784 
mutations with experimentally determined ΔΔG’s for 80 proteins, in which the mutations are not included 
for training the particular method. This shows that the 2 methods perform quite well, but not perfect. 
Another reference160 report the accuracy of the FoldX and Popmusic to be 0.75 and 0.85 respectively. I.e. the 
accuracy largely depends on the evaluation dataset.  
A disadvantage of structure-based approaches is that they are limited to stability effects166.  
 
5.3 Statistics 
The statistics used in this project consist of R2 values, p-values, averages and standard deviations (SD). A 
confidence interval of 95%  has been used. Thus, correlations with a p-value larger than 0.05 are rejected.

R2 is the proportional of variance in the dependent variable explained by the linear regression 178. 
Initially, p-values was calculated in excel, but as the data set became very large, it was easier to calculate 
the p-values in MATLAB, using the MATLAB function ttest2(). p-values “are the probability of obtaining a 
value to the test statistics that is as extreme or more extreme than the value actually observed” 178. In other 
==PAGE:59==
 
59 
 
words, a small p-value means that there is an association between the x and the y variable. The p-value is 
calculated under the 0-hypothesis. I.e. the p-value is the probability of finding the observed results (or 
more extreme results) when the 0-hypotehsis is true.  
The p-value is also used to accept or reject the 0-hypothesis. The 0-hypothesis used in this project, is that 
the mean values of the two samples are equal. I.e. a p-value below 0.05, rejects this 0-hypothesis, and 
states that the two samples have a significant difference in mean value. 
The p-value can be calculated by calculating the Z-value and then use a standard normal distribution 
function to calculate p by p=2F(z), in which

5.5 Measuring the pathogenicity of point mutations 
The concept of pathogenicity of mutations can be variating and sometimes uncertain. Sometimes, the word 
pathogenic are used in this thesis for all the mutations found in the clinical mutation data set, even though 
they are not confirmed to be pathogenic. A pathogenic mutation is one that are certain to be pathogenic 
for the disorder, i.e. having the pathogenic mutation will always generate the disease at some point in life. 
For Parkin the general results of the analysis are unclear, so a pathogenic classification of the disease-
associated mutations is made, in order to possibly obtain more statistically significant results by only using 
these mutations. Two methods have been conducted in order to classify Parkin mutations as being 
pathogenic: 
1: A clinical parkin mutation is classified as being pathogenic if 5 or more of 9 pathogenicity predicting 
programs predicts them to be pathogenic.  
2: A clinical parkin mutation is classified as pathogenic if it has at least three individuals with a documented 
age of onset.  
PS-1 and superoxide dismutase [Cn-Cu] already show statistically significant results, so it is assumed that all 
the disease-associated mutations in the dataset are pathogenic (although this might not be true). The 
results for Tau and Prion are not expected to be improved by only using mutations classified as being 
pathogenic. 
 
Computational evidence for pathogenicity with 9 programs 
Mutations for Parkin are inputted into 9 sequence-based programs that predict the pathogenicity of the 
given mutation from the database. These are seen in the table below: 
Method 
Description 
Poly-Phen-2 
Naive Bayes classifier trained by  HumDiv and HumVar dataset 
SIFT 
Uses sequence homology (PSI-BLAST) combined with known 
generic likelihood of amino acid substitutions 
PROVEAN 
Uses pairwise sequence alignment scores (BLAST) 
PANTHER 
A statistical method for scoring the functional likelihood of 
different amino acid substitutions 
PHD-SNP 
Support vector machine based classifier trained by SwissProt 
dataset 
SNAP 
Neural network based classifier trained by experimentally 
obtained variant functional effect data 
SNAP2 
Neural network based classifier trained by experimentally 
obtained variant functional effect data 
META-SNP 
Random forest based meta predictor combining SNAP, SIFT, 
PANTHER and PHD-SNP 
FATHMM 
Hidden Markov Models based classifer 
Table 13 – 9 pathogenic predictor programs for point mutations.  
==PAGE:62==
 
62 
 
The output of the programs are a prediction score and a classification of being “Disease causing” or “Neutral”. 
The prediction scores represent the likelihood of a given mutation causing a deleterious functional change of 
the protein.  
A mutation is then classified as being pathogenic if 5 or more of the programs declares it to be disease-
causing. For optimal results, decision of being pathogenic or neutral, should be weighted according to the 
prediction score, but as the prediction scores have different intervals for the different programs, this is not 
straight forward, and therefore not performed.  
 
A possible guideline to classify mutations as being pathogenic 
Several classification and guidelines of pathogenicity can be made. One of them is referred in the article 180. 
This reference, tells that a mutation can be classified as pathogenic if it fulfills criteria (i), (ii) or (iii) 
according to Figure 29  
 
Figure 29 – 3 ways in which a mutation can be classied as pathogenic according to 180. 
These 3 criteria contain some categories. The relevant categories with respect to missense mutations in this 
project, for  “Very strong”, “Strong”,  “moderate” and “Supporting” are: 
 “Strong” 
- 
PS1: Same amino acid change as previously established pathogenic variants regardless of 
nucleotide change (different nucleotide changes can lead to the same type of mutation).  
- 
PS2: De novo (both maternity and paternity confirmed) in a patient with the disease and no family 
history. (i.e. the mutation is present on both alleles).  
- 
PS3: Well-established in vitro or in vivo functional studies supportive of a damaging effect 
on the gene or gene product 
“Moderate” 
- 
PM1: Located in a mutational hot spot and/or critical and well-established functional domain (e.g., 
active site of an enzyme) without benign variation 
==PAGE:63==
 
63 
 
- 
PM2: Absent from controls (or at extremely low frequency if recessive) in Exome Sequencing 
Project, 1000 Genomes Project, or Exome Aggregation Consortium 
- 
PM5: Novel missense change at an amino acid residue where a different missense change 
determined to be pathogenic has been seen before 
“Supporting” 
- 
PP1: Cosegregation with disease in multiple affected family members in a gene definitively known 
to cause the disease 
- 
PP2: Missense variant in a gene that has a low rate of benign missense variation and in which 
missense variants are a common mechanism of disease 
- 
PP3: Multiple lines of computational evidence support a deleterious effect on the gene or 
gene product (conservation, evolutionary, splicing impact, etc.) 
- 
PP4: Patient’s phenotype or family history is highly specific for a disease with a single genetic 
etiology 
- 
PP5 Reputable source recently reports variant as pathogenic, but the evidence is not available to 
the laboratory to perform an independent evaluation 
Even though PM1, PM2 (few PM5), PP1, PP2, PP3 and PP4 can be fulfilled in many of the cases, it is not 
possible to fulfill the criteria of being pathogenic for most of the point mutation in this project, because it 
requires a strong criterion as well. The only possible strong criteria to be fulfilled, is PS3-criteria for 
Pressinilin-1. A study of 138 mutations associated with AD has been made114, in which mutations having a 
42
40
A
A


 ratio above 1 are seen to be correlated with disease. This provide a way to study each mutation of 
PSEN-1 and see whether they fulfill criteria PS3. But as PS-1 mutations do seem to correlate with disease, 
this is not performed. 
 
 
5.6 Outlier analysis 
 
In order to investigate outliers from the 3 ΔΔG methods methods, and to see whether insignificant analysis 
can become significant by removing outliers, an outlier analysis has been performed. This has been done only 
for Parkin, Prion and Tau protein, as these proteins showed many insignificant p-values in the t-test between 
the mean value of the clinical mutation data sets and the non-conserved mutation data sets. 3 different 
outlier analysis has been performed.  
 
5.6.1 Outlier analysis 1 – Removal of lowest values 
Outliers of the clinical mutation dataset has been taken as all the negative ΔΔG values, as these goes against 
the main hypothesis, that the clinical mutations are destabilizing. 
A t-test with these outliers removed, are then performed.  
This is made for Parkin, Prion and Tau.  
 
==PAGE:64==
 
64 
 
5.6.2 Outlier analysis 2 – Removal of mutations with the trends of outliers 
An analysis based on the removal of mutations with certain trends of the outliers is performed. This is only 
conduced for Parkin, as the tau and Prion analysis are not suspected to benefit from this. 
For Parkin, the 10 outliers for each pdb-structures with the lowest pdb-values was gathered, giving a total of 
30 outliers for each ΔΔG-method. These 30 outliers (some might be the same mutations) are collected in 
tables and histograms, and analyzed for changes in charge, hydrophobicity, size and special mutations such 
as introduction or removal of glycine and proline. And furthermore, the outliers are plotted in the pdb-
structure in Pymol, to check if they localize in certain sites.  
For each ΔΔG-method used on the specific proteins, some trends are seen. All mutations in the clinical 
mutation dataset, having these trends are then removed, and a t-test is performed. At least 4 outliers are 
needed, in order to be regarded as a trend. A Matlab script has been performed to only plot histograms, with 
trends having 4 or more outliers. This script is very long so only a part of the script has been attached in 
Appendix C and called “Outlieranalysis.m”. The matlab script for subtraction of the outliers, are given as an 
example for FoldX with pdb: 4i1h, in Appendix C and called “Extraction_of_outliers.m”.  
In order to investigate increases and decreases in hydrophobicity and size changes, the rankings in Table 14 
and Table 15 respectively have been used. 
The molecular weights (MW) of the amino acids are approximately proportional to their size 181. Therefore, 
the molecular weight has been used a ranking for the sizes.  
The hydrophobicity scale, is “Whimley-White whole residue hydrophobicity scale”, which is experimentally 
determined transfer energies for each amino acid from water to a bilayer interface

They include the contribution from both the peptide bond and the site chain. A greater transfer energy means 
that the amino acid has a harder time going to the bilayer interface, i.e. the amino acid is more hydrophobic 
(than another one).

The mean experimental error for the “hydrophobicity value” (transfer energies) is 0.071 kcal/mol.  
A hydrophobicity increase or decrease, is only regarded when the transfer energies changes with more than 
2 times this mean error. Otherwise, the change is called “similar”. Likewise, a decrease or increase in size is 
only regarded when the change is larger than 1 g/mol. Otherwise, the change is called “similar”.  
When analyzing the outliers for change in hydrophobicity and size, the minimum absolute change of 
hydrophobicity and size is saved. Afterwards, when removing the mutations having the particular trend, for 
example neutral to neutral mutations having an increase in size, only outliers having a greater size-increase, 
than this outlier having the lowest size-increase, are removed.   
For a given charge change, only that trend with most outliers are used. Furthermore, it is desired to have as 
much information as possible. For example, if natural to natural charged mutations has the same number of 
outliers for both a size increase, and an increase in both hydrophobicity and size, the outliers with the change 
in both hydrophobicity and size are chosen as the trend.  
 
==PAGE:66==
 
66 
 
5.7 Outlier analysis 3 – Control data set + Clinical data set 
The final outlier analysis is a combination of outlier analysis 2, and an outlier analysis of the control data set.  
For each of the 3 ΔΔG-method’s, this outlier analysis of the control data set, picks out the 100 greatest ΔΔG 
values for each pdb-file, from the least conserved control data set (i.e. with all animals). The greatest 
numbers are picked as outliers, as these goes against the hypothesis that clinical mutations are more 
destabilizing (and have a higher ΔΔG value) than mutations in non-conserved residues. 
Then an analysis of these 300 outliers are made, for each method, to obtain some trends in the outliers. 
Lastly, all mutations in the 3 control sets having these trends, are removed. Furthermore, the residues from 
the clinical mutation data set with the trend in outlier analysis 2, are removed from the clinical data set. 
The least conserved control set for Parkin induced 4731 mutations for the podb files 5c1z and 5c23 and 3496 
mutations for pdb 4i1h. I.e. 2-3% of the total mutations are picked as outliers.   
 
6 Results 
 
6.1  Computed vs. experimental ΔΔG values 
33 experimental ΔΔG values for 33 mutations of SOD1, are plotted against their respective computed 
values from the 3 methods.  
 
Figure 30 – Computed vs. experimental ΔΔG for the 3 methods for SOD1.  
==PAGE:67==
 
67 
 
Figure 30 show that all methods except foldX on pdb structure 1spd, have a significant linear relationship 
between the computed and experimental ΔΔG values. I.e., the methods are generally able to compute 
estimates of the experimental values with a high significance. Furthermore, the last 8 plots (except the non-
significant one) show a similar slope, indicating consensus between the 3 methods in prediction ΔΔG.

of FoldX values above 5 kcal/mol are also made, which is shown in blue. Red numbers indicate a p-value 
above 0.05, i.e. that the mean values from the population cannot be assumed to be different.

the mean value.  
In Table 16 the p-values for FoldX and Popmusic with the control sets are significant. I.e. the mean value for 
the pathogenic mutations is significantly greater than the mean value for the control sets. This suggest that 
the pathogenic mutations are more destabilizing than mutations in non-conserved residues, for PSEN1, using 
FoldX and Popmusic. 
Non-significant p-values are seen for I-mutant. This agrees with Figure 34 in which the pathogenic and control 
set distributions are very similar. 
For FoldX with outliers, a quite large structure dependence is seen: 5a63 and 5fn3 has similar distributions, 
while 6iyc derivates a lot from those two. For FoldX without outliers, this structure dependence is much 
smaller. 
For Imutant and popmusic, the structure dependence is minimal.  
==PAGE:70==
 
70 
 
The method dependence is very small between the pathogenic Imutant and popmusic, i.e. the pathogenic 
dataset shows similar normal distributions with a mean value around 1 kcal/mol. However, in the case of the 
control set for popmusic, the normal distribution is shifted towards lower mean value around 0.5 kcal/mol. 
FoldX without outliers has a similar mean value to popmusic in all cases, but the variance for FoldX is much 
larger than the variance for popmusic.  
Generally, the distributions between the least and most conserved data sets are very similar, suggesting no 
dependence on how much the control set is conserved.

dependence is seen regarding how conserved the control set are.

6.3 Pymol plots of pathogenic mutations residues 
 
The clinical mutations in the different proteins will be mapped in the pdb-files using pymol. Only one pdb-
file for each protein are used (2 pdb’s for tau). The pdb-file that cover most of the pathogenic mutations 
are chosen. If two pdb-files cover the same amount of pathogenic mutations, the pdb file with the lowest 
resolution has been chosen.  
Positions containing pathogenic mutations are bolted in the following tables of functional sites.  
==PAGE:81==
 
81 
 
 
- 
PSEN1 
 
Figure 51 – Mutations in purple in the PS-1 subunit (in green) of the gamma-secretase complex for pdb 6iyc. APP is shown in cyan.  
Figure 51 shows the mutations from the pathogenic mutation dataset, in the PS-1 subunit of the gamma-
secretase complex of pdb file 6iyc.  Mutations are found in all of the 9 transmembrane helixes of PS-1. Both 
mutations close to and further from APP are seen.  
In Unitprot, 6 functional domains are stated:

Table 22 -  184 
 
As seen in Figure 51  and Table 22  the mutations are spread all over the PS-1 subunit. I.e. the mutations do 
not only reside in certain functional sites. This suggest that the individual mutations all could lead to a 
decreased stability of the protein, and this disability causes a dysfunctional protein. 
None of the PTM serine phosphorylation residues has mutations. This is probably due to that such 
mutations would disrupt the normal functional protein. For instance, the phosphorylation reduces the 
progression of apoptosis 185.

SOD1 
 
==PAGE:83==

Figure 52 – SOD1 Dimer – PDB: 2v0a – Chain A green – Chain F cyan – Mutations in magentas shown as sticks on chain A 
 
Sites, regions and motifs Positions (number of 
mutations in the site)

C6(5) and W32 
5  
Table 23 – Functional sites of SOD1 according to 186 
As seen in Figure 52 and Table 23 the pathogenic mutations are spread all over the protein and in 
difference metal binding sites. This shows either that the mutations can affect the functional activity in the 
protein in a lot of different ways, or that the mutations decrease the stability of the protein yielding a 
misfolded and dysfunctional protein.  
Furthermore, mutations are found in both cysteine residues that normally forms a disulfide bond in the WT 
protein. Mutations in these cysteine residues, prevents the disulfide bridge from forming, thereby 
destabilizing the protein.  
Some pathogenic variants have been shown to leading to proteasomal degradation. For example it is 
known that the variants Arg-37, Arg- 46, Arg- 85, Ala 93, Arg-85 and Ala 93 are ubiquitinated by RNF19A 
and MARCH5 which leads to proteasomal degradation 187 188.  
Indeed, it cannot be denied that mutations in SOD1 have more than one disease mechanism. I.e. both by 
mutations decreasing the protein stability leading to dysfunctional proteins, mutations in functional sites 
leading to dysfunctional protein and mutations leading to proteasomal degradation.

of P-H-G-G-G-W-G-Q 
51-91 
1 (in the flexible tail) 
Table 25 – Functional regions and domains of Prion according to Uniprot 190 
25 out of the 30 pathogenic mutations from the dataset, are found in the globular domain, the rest is found 
in the flexible tail. These mutations 25 in the globular domain are spread all over the domain, as seen in 
Figure 54. Furthermore, Table 25 shows that none of the pathogenic mutations are seen in functional sites 
in the globular domain. I.e. because the globular domain has a secondary role in the function of PrP (the 
flexible tail is responsible for binding to binding partners), a destabilization decrease of the globular domain 
could alter the function of the PrP-binding partner complex while bound, leading to disease.   
 
- 
Tau  
The pdb files for the tau protein cover a limited amount of the pathogenic mutations. Therefore, two 
structures are shown, to cover most of the mutations.

Table 26 – Tau -  Pathogenic mutation sites are bold. 191 
As seen on Figure 55 and Figure 56 the mutations in tau do not seem to cluster in certain regions. 
Furthermore, as seen in Table 26, the mutations do not seem to reside in certain functional sites. As Tau is 
an IDPs, and the pathogenic mutations resides in the IDP domains, the pathogenic mechanism of Tau is 
most likely not due to destabilization of tau, because destabilization disordered domains do not generally 
alter the structure of IPDs, as no 3D structure are present anyway.  
No mutations in the cysteine residue creating the disulfide bond between 291 and 322 are found in the 
literature. If pathogenic mutations always are destabilization, one would suspect that mutations in this 
disulfide bond was present, to destabilize the structure.  
 
 
6.4 Regression between methods 
 
PSEN1 
==PAGE:88==
 
88 
 
 
In all cases a p-value below 0.05 is seen, showing a statistically significant linear relationship between the 
ΔΔG values computed between the 3 different methods, for all 3 structures, for PSEN1. Furthermore, the 
plots show a very similar slope, indicating consensus between the 3 methods. I.e. the methods show similar 
results for the 3 pdb-structures.   
 
 
SOD1 
==PAGE:89==
 
89 
 
 
In all cases exept 1, a p-value below 0.05 is seen. Therefore, it can be concluded that a statistically 
significant linear relationship exists between the ΔΔG values computed between the 3 different methods, 
for all 3 structures, for SOD1. Furthermore, the plots show a similar slope, indicating consensus between 
the 3 methods. I.e. the methods show similar results. 
 
 
Parkin 
 
==PAGE:90==
 
90 
 
 
In all cases, a p-value below 0.05 is seen. Therefore, it can be concluded that a statistically significant linear 
relationship exists between the ΔΔG values computed between the 3 different methods, for all 3 
structures, for Parkin. Furthermore, the plots show a similar slope, indicating consensus between the 3 
methods. I.e. the methods show similar results.  
 
- 
Prion 
==PAGE:91==
 
91 
 
 
Only in two of the cases, a p-value below 0.05 is seen. Therefore, there are generally no statistically 
significant linear relationship between the ΔΔG values computed between the 3 different methods, for all 3 
structures, for Prion. Furthermore, the slopes are very different: some are negative, some nearly constant 
and come har positive. This shows non-consensus between the 3 methods, showing different results for the 
3 methods.    
 
 
- 
Tau 
==PAGE:92==
 
92 
 
 
Only in four of the cases, a p-value below 0.05 is seen. Therefore, there are generally no statistically 
significant linear relationship between the ΔΔG values computed between the 3 different methods, for all 3 
structures, for Tau. Furthermore, the slopes are quite different. This shows non-consensus between the 3 
methods, showing different results for the 3 methods.

6.7.3 Results of outlier analysis 3 
 
 
Neutral to neutral mutations with an increase in size. Almost all of these are C, G, A or S into amino acids at 
least as big as lysine. Thus, this is the trend.   
 
 
 
The histogram shows neutral to neutral/negative mutations with a decrease in hydrophobicity. By further 
analyzing, the neutral to neutral mutations are mainly  F, W or Y into P, G, D or E so this is regarded as one 
of the trends. 
Furthermore, mutations with  V into either D, E, P, G, K, N, S or Q comprise 104 out of the 300 outliers, so 
these are regarded as a trend as well. 
 
==PAGE:101==
 
101 
 
 
Although, by analyzing the 300 outliers, it is seen that V, L and I contributes respectively 117, 65 and 64 of 
the total 300 outliers, with mutations into all amino acid types except L, I, V and M. Therefore, it is assumed 
that I-Mutant tends to overestimate the ΔΔG-value estimation of these types, and these are seen as the trend 
of outliers for I-mutant.

7 Discussion 
 
Decreased stability of SOD1 and PSEN1 due to point mutations are correlated with disease.  
The results for SOD1 shows that the mean values are statistically significant greater, than the mean values 
for all 3 types of control set. Furthermore, a consensus is seen between the 3 ΔΔG methods, from the 
regression plots and the histograms. It is extremely unlikely, that such different methods and pdb-files, all 
can give the same results. Neither changes in  protein-structure, conserveness of the control set or ΔΔG 
methods, changes the results. Furthermore, the clinical mutations are spread all over the protein. Therefore, 
it is concluded that pathogenic SOD1 mutations are more destabilizing than mutations in non-conserved 
residues. And that destabilization of super oxide dismutase [Zn-Cu] is highly correlated with ALS. This is 
already seen in the literature192–196 and in which stability changes are seen to correlate with ALS patient 
survival times. And strategies for destabilizing superoxide dismutase are proposed 197 
 
The results for PS-1 shows that the mean values are statistically significant greater, than the mean values for 
FoldX and popmusic, in all types of control sets. I-Mutant show non-statistically significant p-values, because 
the mean value for the pathogenic data set are very close to the mean value for the control set for I-Mutant. 
However, the regression plots show that a consensus is seen between the 3 ΔΔG methods, i.e. they tend to 
give the same results. Furthermore, an explanation of the non-statistically significant result for I-Mutant 
could be due to that PS-1 is a membrane protein, I-Mutant seems to perform poorly on membrane protein, 
as discussed later in this chapter. Moreover, the clinical mutations are spread all over the protein.  Therefore, 
it is concluded that pathogenic PS-1 mutations are more destabilizing than mutations in non-conserved 
residues. And that destabilization of PSEN-1 due to point mutations causes AD. This is already seen in the 
literature 198,199 in which age of onset carrying PSEN1 mutations are correlated with loss of stability (R2=0.75) 
198.  
 
 
==PAGE:105==
 
105 
 
 
 
Familial Parkinson’s disease might be due to decreased stability of point mutations 
The initial analysis of all Parkin mutations showed that only in 4 cases out of 36, the mutations in the non-
conserved residues are more destabilizing than the pathogenic mutations. However, only in 12 of the 36 
cases, the pathogenic mutations are statistically significantly lower than mutations in non-conserved 
residue. However, the regression plots show that a consensus is seen between the 3 ΔΔG methods, i.e. they 
are giving the same result. Therefore, further analysis was made on Parkin.  
First of all, the validity of pathogenic mutations was investigated. Only mutations having 2 or more patients 
with a confirmed age of onset, was used. This gave a much worse result, in which fewer statistically 
significant values are seen. This is probably due to that age of onset data are very noisy, so a criterion of 
pathogenicity based on age of onset data alone are not sufficient.  
An analysis was made, in which 4 animals having a bad impact on the multiple sequence alignment was 
removed from the alignment. And only Parkin associated disease mutations in which at least 5 out of 9 
pathogenic predictor methods has declared the mutation as being pathogenic. The combination of these 
two approached, gave the results that Popmusic showed statistically significant larger ΔΔG values for the 
pathogenic mutations compared to mutations in non-conserved residues, for all 3 pdb-structures and all 3 
methods. I-Mutant only showed one statistically insignificant p-value. And FoldX showed 8 out 12 of 
insignificant p-values for the main hypothesis. In all cases the mean value for the pathogenic mutations are 
greater than for the mutations in non-conserved sites.  
This leads to the conclusion that loss of stability of Parkin mutations most probably are causing Parkinson 
disease. Although to be sure, further investigations has to be made. For example, by showing a correlation 
between clinical data such as age of onset and loss of stability.    
Previous literature121 already indicates that loss of stability of Parkin are correlated with Parkinson disease, 
but the data are to sparse to make a conclusion. 
 
Loss of stability of prion and tau do not seem be casual for their associated diseases.  
The analysis of tau suffers from the lack of many residues in the pdb-files, giving a very small mutation data 
set, with lack of critical mass. The mean and p-values for the tau analysis, goes in all directions. Sometimes 
the pathogenic mutations are more destabilizing, and sometimes the mutations in the control sets are more 
destabilizing. These variating results are also seen on the regression plots, which have quite different slopes 
and few significant p-values, meaning that the methods show different results and are statistical non-
correlated. The analysis of Prion shows the similar thing.  
The general non-statistically significant results for tau and prion with respect to the main hypothesis, can be 
due to at least 3 reasons: 
1: The lack of well-defined structures and lack of critical mass in data.  
The tau structures lack many of the resides. Therefore, many interactions in the native protein are not 
provided in the pdb-files, giving different results from the 3 different pdb-structures.  This can be seen from 
the histograms, in which the histograms vary a lot for the 3 different pdb-structures. Furthermore, the ΔΔG 
==PAGE:106==
 
106 
 
methods very a lot, most probably because they capture different variations in the very different pdb-
structures. 
The pdb-structures for Prion only include the globular domain. And most mutations in Prion seen in FCJD, are 
seen in this globular domain. But it is the disordered tail that is important for binding. I.e. mutations that 
destabilizes the globular domain, might not causal for disease, because the disordered tail can still bind to 
the binding partners and perform its function. Probably the pathogenic mutations in the globular domain 
effects the function of the prion-binding partner complex, while bound, but this needs to be further 
investigated. The mutations in the globular domain of tau, might as well be casual for disease, although the 
results do not show that. The lack of critical mass of Prion protein mutations found in FCJD, might be the 
reason why are loss of stability isn’t seen to be correlated with disease. Otherwise destabilizing mutations in 
the globular domain of Prion, might be a reason for disease in some case, but not the only way mutations 
can cause CJD. It is likely that CJD can be caused by destabilization of PrP, which causes PrP to misfold and 
aggregate and therefore can’t performs its proper function. And that other non-destabilizing mutations can 
cause CJD in other ways.  
 
2: The result could also be a result of that loss of stability in tau and prion, not are the reason for disease.  
As Tau and Prion are IPD’s, they have many folded conformations and are most often not found in their global 
minimum. I.e. Tau and Prion are differentiating between different states and different stabilities, when 
performing their different functions with different binding partners. Therefore, destabilization of IDP’s 
probably has no effect on the NDD’s, because destabilization just makes IDP’s more disordered, but they can 
still bind to their many binding partners and performs their functions (IPD’s do not have a tertiary structure 
in their disordered domains).  
In order to make a final conclusion on Prion and tau, and on IDP’s in general, an analysis on a larger number 
of pathogenic mutations needs to be made, in order to investigate whether pathogenic mutations in IDP’s 
are significantly more stabilizing than mutations in non-conserved residues. Furthermore, better structures 
of IDP’s need to be made, and structures of Tau in complex with microtubule, and Prion in complex with 
diverse binding partners.  
 
3: pathogenic mutations in Prion are stabilizing 
As a matter of fact, the result for prion show that the majority of the clinical point mutations are more 
stabilizing than the mutations in the control sets. Thus, could lead to the suggestion that stabilizing of Prion 
protein, leads to disease. As Prion is an IPD, it needs to be able to be destabilized in order to go into different 
conformations. Such a hypothesis seems plausible. But as the clinical mutations lack critical mass, and as the 
results are very sensible to changes in ddG method, control set and protein structures and as the analysis 
only is conducted on the globular domain of Prion, this hypothesis is most likely not true.  
 
Solvent accessibility 
PS-1 are found in the transmembrane area in the cell. But the ΔΔG methods uses pdb-files in which the 
residue on the surface of the protein structure are assumed to be in touch with aqueous solvent. Therefore, 
an incorrect solvent accessibility of the surface residues is made, which causes mistakes for these residues.  
==PAGE:107==
 
107 
 
Furthermore, the hydrophobic transmembrane residues are in unfavorable interactions with the solvent in 
the pdb-files, leading to that mutations into hydrophilic residues are stabilizing. To make this worse, the 3 
ΔΔG methods used in this project, are all trained on experimental mutations in solutions in vitro, from the 
Protherm database. As ~1 of the proteins in https://www.rcsb.org/ are membrane proteins, not many 
membrane proteins are assumed to be included in the protherm database, and membrane proteins are often 
only souble when adding additives such as certain polymers. This is discussed in the article200, in which 10 
ΔΔG methods including FoldX and I-Mutant all performs worse on membrane proteins comparted to globular 
soluble proteins. In the article, FoldX has a peason correlation coefficient (PCC) equal to 0.8 while I-Mutant 
have a PCC=0.69. 
Figure 61 show the the 10 outliers for each pdb for Imutant, both for the control set and the pathogenic data 
set. 
 
Figure 61 – PS-1 in Cyan and AP-1, PEN-2 and APP in green. Outlires from control set are in red, and outliers from pathogenic data 
set are in purple.  
As one might see from Figure 61, almost all of the outliers for the pathogenic dataset residues are directed 
towards the inner of the structure, while the outliers for the control data set are directed outwards towards 
the solvent. As the outliers for the control data set are the largest ΔΔG values (the most destalizing), I-Mutant 
might overestimate the effects of these mutations, because the algorithm is trained to be subject to water. 
These mutations, and the fact that I-Mutant performs worse than FodlX on membrane proteins200,   might be 
the reason why the analysis using I-Mutant on PS-1 seems to have non-significant p-values, for the 0-
hypotehsis of different mean values. And why FoldX have significant ones.  
On the other hand, SOD1 resides in the cell cytosol in the body, i.e. is sounded by aqueous solvent. Therefore, 
the ΔΔG methods are optimized to such globular proteins, and perform well on such proteins. 
==PAGE:108==
 
108 
 
 
In vivo and in vitro 
As said, all the 3 ΔΔG prediction methods, are trained on experimental mutations in solutions in vitro. 
Therefore, they might not capture some important features of the proteins, because they all are in vivo, 
leading to uncertain ΔΔG estimates. 
 
Combination of methods 
The combination of the 3 ΔΔG methods, was performed in order to capture different characteristics of the 3 
methods in one model. The combined model showed better results on the experimental ΔΔG data set than 
any of the 3 single models. But performed similar or slightly worse, on the Parkin dataset, given that the 
decreased stability hypothesis is correct.  
This is most likely due to that the model has been trained only on SOD1 mutations on the 3 pdb-files for 
SOD1. I.e. the model is most likely overfitted to these pdb-files, and does not perform well in general, because 
noise from the pdb-files has been captured in the model as well.  
In order to obtain a universal well-performing model, one could use a dataset of experimental ΔΔG mutations 
for a huge amount of proteins (such as the Protherm database) and train the model to these proteins. 
Overfitting should be avoided such that the model only learns the trends in the data, but not also the noise 
in the dataset. Overfitting can be avoided by using cross-validation, i.e. alternation picking out a random part 
of the model, use that to train the model, and then evaluate on the remaining dataset. Once the error starts 
to increase on the remaining dataset, one should stop training. Then a different part of the dataset is picked, 
and the rest are used as evaluation. This is repeated x-times and are called x-fold-cross validation. Then x-
models are obtained, and one can use the mean of these models as the final one.  
 
Age of onset do not correlate with decreased stability 
Appendix B shows plots of age of onset vs. ΔΔG methods for PSEN1, SOD1, Parkin and Prion. Almost no 
statistically significant relationships are seen between these. I.e. Age of onset do not correlate with the 
stability of point mutations for any of the 3 ΔΔG methods or pdb-structures. This is probably due to that the 
age of onset are subject to a lot of noise because many other factors that only protein stability contributes 
to the disease onset.  Furthermore, it was tried to only include data having at least x number of 
documented patients with age of onset data, with different numbers of x, but this gave very few 
statistically significant relationships as well.   
Note that no plot of tau exists, because the data are too sparse.  
In previous literature,  the loss of stability of SOD1 has seen to be correlated with survival time 192. And the 
loss of stability of PSEN1 has seen to be correlated with age of onset, for pathogenic mutations having a 
sufficient decrease in the 
42
40
/
A
A


 ratio 198. 
 
==PAGE:109==
 
109 
 
8 Conclusion 
 
From this project, it can be concluded that the loss of stability of PS-1 and superoxide dismutase[Cu-Zn] are 
contributing to Alzheimer’s disease and amyotrophic lateral sclerosis respectively.  All p-values for different 
methods for SOD1 is significant, and almost all p-values for PS-1 is significant. The 3 very different ΔΔG 
predicting methods used to obtain these results, all showed consensus in computing the ΔΔG values for the 
different pdb-structures. Furthermore, the results showed no sensitivity to changes in the control sets. This 
makes the conclusion more reliable, because it is highly unlikely that such different methods all obtain the 
same result, in different pdb-structures and control data sets.  
It can also be concluded that destabilizing of Parkin seems to cause Parkinson disease, although further 
analysis needs to be made in order to make a final conclusion on this. The initial analysis of Parkin showed 
results directing in different directions, but with the removal of potential alignment outliers and by 
constraining the clinical mutation data set to only include mutations classified as being pathogenic based 
on 9 pathogenic mutations prediction methods, the conclusion of Parkin being a causal factor for 
Parkinson’s disease seems very reliable.    
Lastly, it is concluded that the loss of stability for Prion and Tau do not seem to cause CJD and FTD 
respectively. Maybe the destabilizing mutations in the globular domain of CJD are the reason for disease, 
but many of the mutations associated  with CJD are not destabilizing to Prion, so it does not seem to be the 
case  for all mutations. This tells that another pathogenic mechanism might be the case, or that complex 
CJD can be obtained in different possible ways. However, the result of Tau and Prion are subject to missing 
residues in the pdb-files, which could provide unreliable results. I.e. further investigations need to be 
performed, in order to make a final conclusion upon whether loss of stability of Prion and Tau are causing 
CJD and FTD respectively. 
Overall, it can be concluded that, loss of stability of proteins are causing neurodegenerative disease for 
some but not all proteins. It seems to cause disease for globular and membrane proteins, but not for IDP’s.

Appendices 
Appendix A  -  Pathogenic mutations database 
The following 5 subsections includes the pathogenic dataset and their references for the 5 NDDs of subject.   
In the tables in this appendix, n is the number of individuals having the mutations and the given disease.  
AAD is the average age of death. AST is the average survival time. No of fam. is the unique number of 
families in which in mutations are seen in. And family hist. states whether a family history has been seen 
associated with the mutation and disease.

Appendix B    Age of onset as a function of ΔΔG 
Linear regression is performed between the age of onset and the ΔΔG values. This is performed for each 
method, and for each structure. 
 
- 
PSEN1 
==PAGE:154==
 
154 
 
 
No p-values is seen below 0.05. Therefore, there is not statistically significant relationship between age of 
onset and ΔΔG for PSEN1.  
 
- 
SOD1 
==PAGE:155==
 
155 
 
 
No p-values is seen below 0.05. Therefore, there is not statistically significant linear relationship between 
age of onset and ΔΔG for PSEN1.  
 
 
- 
Parkin 
==PAGE:156==
 
156 
 
 
Only in one case, there is a p-value below 0.05. Therefore, there is not statistically significant linear 
relationship between age of onset and ΔΔG for Parkin. 
 
 
- 
Prion 
==PAGE:157==
 
157 
 
 
In all cases the p-value is above 0.05. Therefore, there is not statistically significant linear relationship 
between age of onset and ΔΔG for Prion.
